# Igros Kodesh

## Volume 11

## 3681

1 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn

To the esteemed and distinguished individual, engaged in communal affairs, Moreinu HaRav Moshe, may he live, HaKohen.

Greetings and blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your lines, in which you write about yourself and your wife, may she live. I will mention you both at an *eis ratzon* at the holy *tziyun* of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, *ztzukullehah, navgam, zya*, for whatever you may need in both material and spiritual matters.

Your contribution, a gift of your heart, since it arrived in the days before Pesach, has been directed to the relevant matter—*kimcha d'pischa* (wheat flour for Pesach).

It is a well-known adage of the Baal Shem Tov, which we have heard many times from my father-in-law, the Rebbe, that from everything one must learn a lesson in the *avodah* of man to his Creator, which is the purpose of his creation, as our Sages of blessed memory state at the end of *Kiddushin*: "I was created only to serve my Creator."

This is all the more so regarding matters of Torah and *mitzvos*. In the present case, the custom of Israel is Torah, and it is brought in the *Rishonim* that before Pesach, one collects *kimcha d'pischa*—that is, assistance for the poor.

This is also the introduction to the *Seder* and the text of the *Haggadah*, which begins with the matter of "All who are hungry, let them come and eat." The specific lesson and emphasis here is that all of Israel are responsible for one another, both in material matters and in spiritual matters. It is impossible for a person to be a *ben chorin* (a free man) and to conduct himself in a manner of freedom during the days of Pesach—as the Torah demands, calling this time "the season of our freedom," and especially at the time of conducting the *Seder*—unless he does everything in his power to ensure that his fellow Jew is also a *ben chorin*, free and exempt from worries. On the contrary, one must prioritize the needs of his fellow before beginning the celebration of his own freedom. Therefore, the introduction to the celebration of "the season of our freedom" is the matter of *ma'os chitim*, and the explicit introduction to conducting the *Seder* is the announcement: "All who are hungry, let them come and eat."

From this, we can also learn regarding the *neshamah* and the body: it is impossible for the *neshamah* to be a *ben chorin*—a servant to Hashem and not a servant to Pharaoh—unless it also liberates the body, so that the body, too, is a servant of Hashem and not a servant of Pharaoh. This is the lesson of the Baal Shem Tov in his well-known teaching: "If you see the donkey of your enemy..."—when you look with careful insight at your "materiality" (*chomer*), which is the body, you will see that it is an "enemy" to the *neshamah* that yearns for *Elokus* and spirituality. Furthermore, you will see that it is "crouching under its burden"—the burden that the *Ribono shel Olam* gave to the body, that it should be refined through Torah and *mitzvos*, yet the body is sluggish in fulfilling them. You might think, "You shall surely refrain from leaving it alone"—that it is possible to fulfill your mission by starting with self-affliction to abandon materiality. However, that is not the path through which the light of Torah will dwell. Rather, "You shall surely help him"—to clarify the body and refine it, not to break it through self-affliction.

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach holiday,

**M. Schneerson**

## 3882

B”H, 1 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn

To my dear relative, the distinguished Chossid, G-d-fearing, and learned,
R’ Shmuel Menachem Mendel, may he live,

Shalom u’Bracha!

In response to your letter of 26 Adar and the one preceding it: as you requested, I will mention all those you wrote about at the holy resting place of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, for whatever they require in both material and spiritual matters.

Regarding what you wrote concerning customs during the first year of mourning, as well as the customs for the Yahrtzeit that contain novel points—most of them, if not all, are brought in the printed kuntrusim for 10 and 13 Shevat and 2 Nissan.

Regarding your question as to whether a mourner leads the davening during Mincha on Erev Shabbos, Erev Pesach, etc.—the general instruction is that the mourner does lead the davening, except on days that include Musaf, during which he does not lead the davening, even for Maariv, etc.

Regarding what you wrote about the inscription on a matzeivah: in general, in these matters, one must be careful regarding the honor of the deceased and the honor of others buried in that cemetery. That is to say, there should be no deviation that could infringe upon the honor of anyone else there. Consequently, if the matzeivos in that cemetery include titles, etc., one should not change the text of this matzeivah. It is understood that, following the above, one should also mention his lineage, etc. (provided, of course, that this does not infringe upon the honor of others). Especially since, once this is carved onto the matzeivah, those who pass by and read it—whether aloud or at least in thought—will also read these words in proximity to the matzeivah and the grave. It is known from the holy Zohar that a word ascends and pierces the heavens, etc., and awakens that which it awakens; if it is good, it is good—as cited in the holy Tanya, Chapter 40.

With a blessing that you, your household, and your entire family may celebrate the Festival of Matzos as commanded by our holy Torah, the Torah of Life,

**M. Schneerson**

## 383

B.H., Rosh Chodesh Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn.

Agudas N'shei U'Bnos Chabad
Branch: Tel Aviv
May they live.

Greetings and blessings!

These are the days before Pesach, which, as our Sages of blessed memory tell us, was the time of the redemption from Egypt in the merit of the righteous women of that generation.

May we all be privileged, very soon, to the complete and true redemption through Mashiach Tzidkeinu. The women of our generation have the ability to hasten and contribute to this, and to a great extent. May Hashem grant you success.

With respect and blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach to each of you and to all your family members—may they live.

Your letter has now been received, along with the enclosure. Organizing the meeting with the mothers of the school in D... is very good indeed. You should certainly continue with such efforts, for it is a strong principle that propaganda [outreach] does not return empty, even if the results are sometimes only seen after some time.

You should surely turn to the administration of Agudas Chabad with a request to provide a speaker or lecturer for your farbrengens—if you feel it is necessary (in my opinion, several of your members are capable of lecturing, etc.). They will certainly fulfill the request.

## 3484

B"H, 2 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing and refined, engaged in communal needs,
Rabbi Shlomo Zalman, may he live and be well,

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter from Erev Rosh Chodesh Nissan, together with the enclosure.

Regarding what you wrote concerning your son’s haircut—whether to postpone it—it is surprising why one would do so. Since it is the custom of Israel to perform the haircut on the day he completes three years, this is an auspicious and successful time. Who knows if, on another day that one might choose, one will encounter such a *eis ratzon* (time of favor)? And although, on the face of it, there is a slight contradiction to this from what is written in *Be’er Heitev* to *Orach Chaim*, section 531, subsection 7, that it is permitted to delay it, etc., that source refers to a case where there is a halachic reason for doing so.

Regarding what you wrote about sending printed materials to rabbis, etc.: sending them to everyone would, on the face of it, not be of much benefit, since they receive many types of publications that they do not even read. If it is possible to clarify which of those on your list are interested in the matters published by Kehot and the like—and, most importantly, if they are also influenced regarding actual practice—then there is room to take an interest and develop a plan—a program—of whom to send to and of what type.

The enclosure regarding *Maos Chittim* has been delivered for its intended purpose, and they will notify you directly.

My previous letter, with the enclosure, you surely received.

With blessings for success in your holy work and in your personal affairs.

#### ***3484***

1 Rabbi Shlomo Zalman: Hecht. Additional letters to him—see above, Vol. 8, 2890, and the references noted in the footnotes there.

**Whether to postpone it:** To Lag B’Omer. See also below, letter 3339.

## 355

B"H, 4 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 24 Adar and the one preceding it.

1) Regarding the procedures of the Yeshiva: Surely you are aware of the Chabad approach, which is not the approach of *mussar*. When words of *hisorerus* are occasionally spoken—even using expressions that do not provide *taanug*—it is not due to a love for preaching *mussar*, but rather in the hope that it will lead to practical action; that through the saying of these things, the situation will change, if indeed a change is required...

Regarding implementing a system of fines or the like for *bochurim* who are negligent, etc.—it depends on the impression it makes and the benefit one can hope to derive from it. One must decide according to the assessment of the situation...

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach.

5) Regarding the distribution of *shmurah* matzah, and the copy of the letter enclosed herewith—it is obvious that this applies this year as well, for the Torah is eternal. To our sorrow and regret, there are still many people in need of the awakening and the campaign discussed in the *sicha*. It is understood that the more one increases the types of recipients of *shmurah*, and the more one is *mehader* in this *shmurah* to the extent possible, the more praiseworthy it is. The promise of the Arizal is already well-known, that one who is careful even with a minute amount of *chametz* during the days of Pesach, etc., and the *mitzvos* of the Seder—and especially the middle matzah—is the essence of this matter, for it is *d'oraysa* even in this time. It is called *michla d'mehemnusa* (food of faith) and *michla d'asvusa* (food of healing), which strengthens faith within each and every one of the children of Israel.

6) Regarding what you wrote concerning the various questions you have about the foundations of our religion and how they can be proven through human intellect: There are many books and *maamarim* published on this. It is surprising that you do not mention even one of them. It is understood that it is difficult to elaborate in a letter and repeat the same things that have been in print for many years. Nevertheless, so that your question does not remain entirely unanswered, I will at least point out a few key points.

a) Do we know the existence of Hashem in a positive or negative way?

**Response:**

It is understood and simple that we know this in a positive way as well. The proof for this is: 1) Just as when one sees a printed book with letters and pages, it is simple to the viewer that there is a printing press where all the letters were arranged in a manner that explains intellectual concepts, etc.—and it is not that ink was spilled and, by mere chance, the drops of ink arranged themselves on hundreds of pages in the form of letters that explain various things. The same is true—only many times over—when one sees a piece of wood, stone, and the like, and subsequently learns from the arrangement of the atoms within them that they are not in the number of hundreds or thousands, but of billions of billions, and all are arranged in a wondrous manner, conforming to fixed and precise laws to the final degree, as is explained at length in *Chovos HaLevavos*, *Kuzari*, and so on. See also *Sefer HaChakirah* by the Tzemach Tzedek. 2) Just as a person knows the existence of souls by seeing the vitality in animals and the like.

b) What is the necessity that the Torah is from Sinai? If it is only because of tradition, then that is also the proof of other religions—to distinguish between the holy and the impure.

**Response:**

It is surprising that you apparently have not read what is printed on this subject in the *Shavuos* booklet of the *Merkaz L'Inyonei Chinuch*, in "Talks and Tales," etc. The distinction in the above is a fundamental one: It is possible for one person to deceive the people of his generation, or even for a few people to conspire together and deceive their generation, or even for a group of people to deceive their generation because they had some **shared** interest for which they wanted to deceive them. This applies to all religions—of the Christians, the Ishmaelites, the people of India, etc.—where the beginning of the religion is that one person told a few of his students that G-d appeared to him and gave him *mitzvos*, instructions, laws, and decrees. This is how it is in all the religions of the world without exception, except for our religion and our Torah. We know about it from the generations before us; those of the children of Israel in our generation heard it from those in the generation before them, and so on, generation after generation, until the generation that stood at Mount Sinai. Six hundred thousand men from the age of twenty to sixty, and in addition to them, millions of young people, elders, women, and the *Erev Rav* heard—**all at once**: "I am Hashem your G-d," etc., not through an intermediary, but from the mouth of the Almighty. And among those six hundred thousand on foot and all the aforementioned were people of various types: *amei ha'aretz* and learned scholars, rich and poor, etc. All of them heard the same phrasing, the same words, and they transmitted this **in the same phrasing** to their children, and their children to their children, until our generation. It is certainly known that all the Reformers and Conservatives began only a few decades ago. Until then, while there were individual *apikorsim*, millions upon millions said the same things in the same phrasing, etc. Throughout **all** the life of the Jewish people, there was not even **one** moment where this tradition was not transmitted by hundreds of thousands of men and women **at once**. There is not even **one** matter in the entire world that has such faithful testimony as the matter of *Matan Torah*.

c) What is the reason that we cannot change the *takanos* of *Chazal*?

**Response:**

By way of introduction, take an example from the science of mathematics: In our day, it is founded on principles that were known and recorded in books even in the days of the early Greeks, Euclid, etc. Even though their books on this science are much smaller than the books of our day, this is because on the foundation of the **rules** and according to the **methods** that were copied from the aforementioned sages, one proceeds from matter to matter and discovery to discovery. However, since this is according to the methods they outlined and the rules they established, it is already included in their words. To distinguish between the holy and the mundane, it is the same with our holy Torah. Rules and instructions were given on how to use these rules and how to derive new conclusions from them. All of this was given at the standing at Mount Sinai, etc., to millions of people at once. Throughout time, the sages in every generation, on the **foundation of these rules**—or in the common parlance, the *midos* by which the Torah is expounded—followed the paths that the **Torah** said are the true paths, and arrived at various conclusions, or in common parlance, various *dinim* and instructions. Even though these *dinim* were stated by such-and-such *Tanna*, *Amora*, or *Gaon*, all of this is included in those general foundations and methods that Moshe Rabbeinu received from the Giver of the Torah Himself. From this, it is understood and simple that if one wishes to solve a question that has arisen in this time—such as a question related to electricity and the like—and one wishes to solve it in the spirit of the **Torah**, as the Rabbis of previous generations solved them, one must follow the paths of the **Torah** and its rules. One of the rules in this is that a *Beis Din* cannot annul the words of another *Beis Din* unless it is greater than it, etc. This, too, is a rule from the **Giver of the Torah**, just as there is also a rule that the majority prevails within that same *Beis Din*, provided they are a *Beis Din* of people who follow the paths of the Torah in these studies.

d) Regarding what is asked if there is holiness in the letters, and if this is written in the *Shulchan Aruch*:

This is written in a book even before the *Shulchan Aruch*, in the *Gemara Shabbos* 104a, and in many places in the holy *Zohar* and *Tikkunei Zohar*. The same applies to the **vowels** (*Tikkunei Zohar*, *Tikkun* 5, 21; see *Zohar Chadash* in *Tikkunim*).

All of the above is simple and most understandable. The fact that there are those who challenge this is only because it is their only way to justify to themselves how they transgress the *mitzvos* of our Torah, the Torah of life. They are ashamed to say that the power of their desire is so great that they cannot stand against it. Consequently, they want to explain it away, remove the yoke from their neck, and cast doubt on the matter itself. In general, one must know clearly that it is one of two things: either one believes in the Torah from Heaven—and then the ruling brought in the Rambam is mandatory, that anyone who says the entire Torah is from Heaven except for one letter is the opposite of the **foundation** of faith—or, if one does not want to accept the instructions of the Torah, one automatically casts doubt also on "I am Hashem your G-d" which is in the Written Torah. Those who believe that Hashem is the Creator of the world only on Yom Kippur and during the time of saying *Yizkor* in the synagogue, but not throughout the year and on weekdays—this is the opposite of common sense. Anyone who believes this in his simplicity belongs in a mental hospital, G-d forbid. Rather, as mentioned, there are people who do not want to fight against their *yetzer* and try to justify the sins they commit through this.

May Hashem grant you success to be a good *shaliach*, so that through you, true faith will be drawn down into the members of your community, especially in these days, the eve of *michla d'mehemnusa* and *michla d'asvusa*, which strengthen the simple and pure faith in every man and woman of Israel, according to the words of our Sages that when a child (intellectually) eats grain food (and as explained in *Chassidus*, this is matzah), he calls out to his Father—our Father in Heaven.

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach.

## 3,787

B"H, 5 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rav, the G-d-fearing, learned, and distinguished, etc.,
Moreinu HaRav Chaim Menachem Shlomo, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of the 27th of Adar, regarding your observation on the Responsa of the Tzemach Tzedek, Yoreh Deah, Siman 104: You question the ruling concerning a doubt as to whether a child was born before or after *shekias hachamah* (sunset), where he rules that since no stars were visible in the sky at the time of birth, it is still considered day. You raise a difficulty: according to the Alter Rebbe in his *Siddur*, *bein hashmashos* begins when the sun has completely set, and after three-quarters of a *mil* it is night; therefore, according to this opinion, the visibility of stars is irrelevant.

However, regardless of how we interpret the *beraisa* regarding "one star is day, two stars is *bein hashmashos*," etc.—whether it applies only to Rabbi Yehuda or also to Rabbi Yosi—when no stars at all are visible in the sky, meaning not even one, and not even the largest ones, it is still considered day (and *shekias hachamah* has not yet occurred). Therefore, the authorities are consistent on this matter, as cited in the aforementioned Responsa. This does not pertain to the dispute between Rabbi Yosi and Rabbi Yehuda, because by the non-visibility of any stars, we clarify the doubt as to whether *shekias hachamah* has occurred or not (when the mountain obscures the west, or in the case of clouds).

With blessings,

**M. Schneerson**

## Gimmel-Shin-Pey-Ches

B"H, 5 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi, the chossid, G-d-fearing, and distinguished, our teacher Rabbi Yosef Baruch, may he live:

Peace and blessing!

It has been some time since I received your letter, and it is surprising that from then until now, there has been no news from you regarding your successful placement—successful both in terms of gashmius and in terms of ruchnius. We are currently in the days that "connect one redemption to another," and the wondrous Rashi is well-known (and I am well aware of how it lives within the language of Rashi) in Taanis 29a, end of the first side, s.v. *Mishenichnas Adar*: "These were days of miracles for Israel, Purim **and Pesach.**" This proves that they are one and the same; on the contrary, they are named after Adar, in which the light of the first and final Redeemer was born and shone—the redemption of Mitzrayim and "As in the days of your exodus from Mitzrayim, I will show him wonders"—in the future redemption. For through the fact that they fulfilled what they had already accepted in the days of Purim in Adar—whereby the "great protest" against the Torah was nullified—the inner soul of the Jewish person, which was already with him in faith (as is known from the Maharal, based on what the Rambam writes in Hilchos Geirushin, end of chapter 2), becomes unified with the outer soul. This is the vessel for the revelation of the unity of the hidden aspects of Torah with the revealed, and for the unity and revelation of the hidden aspects of the Ribono shel Olam with the revealed aspects of the Ribono shel Olam. This is the concept of the complete and true redemption, when "all will **know Me**, from the smallest to the greatest" (Shaar HaEmunah, chapter 17 and onwards).

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach,

**M. Schneerson**

***

**Rabbi Yosef Baruch:** Blumenfeld. Additional letters to him—above, Vol. 7, 1853. Below, 3449.

3451.

**And the wondrous Rashi is well-known:** See also Likkutei Sichos, Vol. 17, p. 344.

## 350

B"H, 5 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

...I was pleased to read that you see a sign of blessing in your work in education. Surely you will not be satisfied with work in this field alone, important as it is, but will do everything in your power to disseminate Yiddishkeit in other areas as well. In this regard, one of the primary tasks is to develop activists for this cause from among the people who are on the spot. Although it is understood that until they become accustomed to it, and regarding their level of reliability and sense of responsibility, they require special supervision and frequent guidance, little by little they become accustomed and improve in their work. Consequently, one should hope that in the near future they will become a help. This is analogous to a teacher, guide, and educator who is truly effective, whose goal is that the student will be able to learn on his own, and furthermore, that he will be one who "moistens others" [i.e., influences others to grow]...

With blessings for success and for good tidings in all the above.

## 350

6 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn

Greetings and blessings!

...The primary point is that in all the aforementioned matters, you seem to rely on your own opinion. As is the way of most people—and indeed the vast majority—you rely on your own opinion, which is to say, your own will. Yet, we see clearly that one’s will and one’s intellect—in the literal sense—are not always one and the same.

Although what follows may seem simple, because this matter concerns what our Sages call "the work of Hashem"—namely, the chinuch of the children of Yisrael—I must reiterate these points, in the hope that it might be of some benefit.

It is a simple and observable fact that every person possesses the trait of self-love. Consequently, it is clear and observable that this natural love leads to corresponding desires, and these desires distort one’s intellect—even the intellect of the person himself. That is to say, were his intellect not biased, he would never have reached such a conclusion, even if he were biased. Indeed, one’s will has the power to sway a person and his intellect far more than the aforementioned bias. The only advice is this: do not rely on your own will, nor even on your own opinion, nor even on your own intellect. Even if it seems to you that these matters are necessary, straightforward, and nothing but pure holiness without any ulterior motive, it is a known ruling of our Sages that "a person cannot declare himself a tzaddik." The test is to hear the opinions of others regarding the matter.

All of the above is true even if we were speaking of ordinary educational institutions. But when, in addition to this, the matter concerns an institution named after my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, *ztzukl"h, nbg"m, zy"a*, the Nasi of Yisrael—and this is no small matter—then it is a source of even greater wonder and astonishment. Who has such broad shoulders to take it upon themselves and say, "**I say**," when all the other members of the institution’s administration say otherwise?

Instead of doing all of this, you acted on your own opinion, surely without revealing it to anyone else beforehand, because you yourself understood that they would not agree to it. You know that the responsibility and the complaints will fall upon the institutions of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, *ztzukl"h, nbg"m, zy"a*, and that the administration of the institution will have to justify your actions and bear the damage, *chas v'shalom*, that may result from this.

**It is imperative** that from here on out, you cease any conduct that involves taking action based solely on the foundation of "I say" and "I desire," for before Him, all is considered as nothing. It would be sufficient to engage in a brief contemplation—even for a short while—of the few words in the holy Tanya, beginning of Chapter 41: "And one must always keep in mind the beginning, essence, and root of all service... to first awaken the natural fear... and behold, Hashem stands over him... and examines his kidneys and heart to see if he is serving Him as is fitting." I am certain that if you had done this, you would not have arrived at all the actions mentioned above, and surely there would have been greater success. Furthermore, not only would there have been no undesirable outcomes, but gradually, the conduct of *Olam HaTohu* would have diminished, and you would have slowly settled into the conduct of *Olam HaTikkun*. If you truly value my opinion in any measure, please inform me in your reply that you have begun to change this conduct of yours in practice. Although it is certainly difficult work to effect such a change, these days of the month of redemption and miracles are capable of redeeming you from this as well. It depends only on the will. May it be the will of Hashem that you do not wish to deceive yourself; then you will see the truth as it truly is, and you will immediately set out to correct these matters.

I would add that it is likely that the *kluginker* (the clever one), as my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, used to say, will approach you with cunning. He will suggest that since your work is not desired, and since you do not find favor in the eyes of your colleagues, and since your opinions do not align, etc., you should therefore resign from your work and occupy yourself with other matters. Therefore, I am adding this response to address that argument from the outset, even though this has been known for some time: one does not run away from Hashem, and one does not run away from our holy Rebbes, *ztzukl"h, nbg"m, zy"a*. If they chose one of *Anash* and granted him the merit—and enabled him, him and his family, to be placed in a beautiful position—this is successful work (even if they see that this is a merit and a success for him, and consequently it makes little difference whether he has already realized this or has not yet attained such an understanding), then it is imperative that he occupy himself specifically with this work. It is also understood and simple that if he only acts in this according to the intention and will of our Nasi, it will surely bring him success in his work, and consequently in all his affairs and the affairs of his family. However, since man has been given free choice, for a certain period of time he has the ability, *chas v'shalom*, not to listen to the will of our Nasi—that is, to act contrary to their will, which is the opposite of their desire, and not to engage in his aforementioned work. But in the end, "no one will be cast away from Him," and our holy Nasi will accomplish their will with kindness and mercy.

As we approach the festival of Matzos, which comes upon us and all of Yisrael for good, and on the first night, whose primary mitzvah is the matzah—the "food of healing"—may they exert their influence upon each and every one of us in their fullness. May this influence be drawn down throughout the entire year, as explained in *Chassidus* (including in the beginning of *Shaar HaEmunah*), in accordance with the statement of our Sages: "Chavakuk came and based them on one, as it is written: 'And the tzaddik shall live by his faith.'"

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach, and for good tidings regarding the above in your reply.

## 3191

B"H, 6 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of the 28th of Adar... It is obvious and simple that to wait to begin the work until the conditions you write about are met—a) that there be complete unity among [the members], etc., and b) that Anash and their institutions be subordinate, etc.—is, in other words, to despair of even beginning the work. The truth is that I have yet to find a period—even during the years of the Nasi, my father-in-law, the Rebbe—when complete unity prevailed, or when Anash and the institutions were subordinate to anyone (with the exception of a significant portion of them, regarding the Nasi himself, my father-in-law, the Rebbe).

The solution to the situation is not necessarily through the aforementioned conditions (which are *hilchasa l'Meshicha*, for then it will be said, "the world will be filled with the knowledge of Hashem," and consequently there will be no time for such matters), but rather through a division of labor. I mean that each person should take upon himself a specific and detailed task—which, of course, must be suited to his ability—and work on it with all his energy. In that matter, he should be the head. It should also be his responsibility to consult with the other members of the executive committee, with decisions made by a majority vote as is customary, and in the most important matters, by asking in a letter and waiting for a response. It is understood that there are some matters that one person cannot fulfill alone, for example, when one needs to visit as a representative on missions, etc. However, once each person has a matter that he is grappling with and sees success in, it will be easier to bring about the submission [to the collective] in the aforementioned matters...

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach—for you and your entire household, may they live—and may the *michla d'meheimanusa* and *michla d'asvusa* [the food of faith and the food of healing] have their full effect on each of them, in both physical and spiritual health together.

**M. Schneerson**

## 3,992

B"H, 7 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn.

To the avreich, David Avraham, may he live,

Greetings and blessings!

After your silence, I was pleased to receive your letter from the month of Adar. I enjoyed reading about your influence on your surroundings—your acquaintances and friends—regarding matters of yiras shamayim, and most importantly, regarding the actual performance of mitzvos in practice. It is certainly unnecessary to remind you not to be discouraged if things do not happen with the desired speed, for we do not know the hidden ways of Hashem. Nevertheless, each and every one of us must do everything that depends on him, and it is a certainty that outreach (taamulah) does not return empty-handed, especially when the words come from the heart, for they enter the heart, even if at times the effect is only seen after some time.

As you requested, I will write to Paris regarding the matter you mention in your letter. May Hashem grant you the merit to succeed and to bring good news regarding this matter and all the aforementioned.

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach and for success in all your affairs,

**M. Schneerson**

**3,992**

## 393

B"H, 8 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the President, the Gabbaim, and the congregants of the Nusach Ari Synagogue, Albany Park,
May Hashem preserve them,

Peace and blessing!

Through the esteemed Rabbi and Chossid, G-d-fearing and upright, engaged in communal needs, etc., Rabbi Shlomo Zalman Hecht, your contribution for *maos chitim* has been received, which is being distributed in our Holy Land—may it be built and established—through our righteous *shluchim*.

One of the lessons of the Yom Tov of Pesach, the time of our freedom, is:

That material freedom—freedom from physical slavery and want—is not, in and of itself, the true goal. It must serve to enable and lead to spiritual liberation, the liberation of the *neshamah*.

This was also the goal of the Exodus from Egypt: "When you bring the people out of Egypt, you shall serve G-d on this mountain"—the giving of the Torah.

This is true for the collective and it is true for the individual. This is why the custom of Israel—which is Torah—is that a Jew begins his "time of our freedom" by performing a *mitzvah*, and a *mitzvah* that is equivalent to all the *mitzvos*: *tzedakah*. He gives for the sake of the needy—*maos chitim*—and before the Seder he declares: "Let all who are hungry come and eat."

In the full sense of the "time of our freedom," I send my blessing that you may enjoy true freedom—freedom from worries in material matters and from worries in spiritual matters, from everything that hinders the *avodah* of Hashem with joy and a glad heart.

And may you continue this joy and freedom throughout the entire year.

With the blessing of a kosher and joyous Pesach,

## 3394

B"H, 8 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 26 Adar: I was pleased to read therein of the improvement in your health, and I am certain you have received my previous letter. May it be the will of Hashem that you soon return to your full strength, both physically and spiritually, in accordance with what is explained in many places in Chassidus, based on the maamar of the Alter Rebbe titled *Maskil L'Eisan HaEzrachi*, which is transcribed at the beginning of the *Kuntres Limud HaChassidus*.

Regarding your question as to whether studying books, sichos, etc., is sufficient for improving one’s conduct and for avodah as it should be—it is understood that this is not the case. Rather, it is necessary for everyone to have someone standing over them. It has already been said, "Make for yourself a rav" (*aseh lecha rav*). As is known, the precise language of *aseh* (make/force) is related to the expression "they compel (*ma'asin*) for tzedakah"—a language of coercion—meaning, even if the student does not desire it. This is especially true when dealing with matters that the teachings of Chassidus address, which are subtle matters by their very nature; consequently, errors in this area are more likely and more damaging.

It is also known how we interpret the words of Moshe, the teacher of all Israel: "Write this as a remembrance in a book, (and nevertheless) place it in the ears of Yehoshua." It is also understood that one does not fulfill one's obligation with just "make for yourself a rav," but there must also be "acquire for yourself a friend" (*keneh lecha chaver*). This means there must be someone to whom one can reveal the afflictions of one's heart, as in the saying of the Mitteler Rebbe, that through this, two nefesh habahamis fight against one.

Regarding your question concerning *kedushah*, etc.: many have the custom in the days before marriage to study the last two chapters of the *Shaar HaKedushah* in *Reishis Chochmah*, and this applies to your question as well.

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach, in good health.

## 3595

B"H, 8 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 21 Adar, in which you write about your family situation and your age—that you are 22 and standing at a crossroads regarding whether to continue in the teaching profession, in which you have begun and succeeded, or to continue your studies in the yeshiva, and you ask for my opinion on this.

According to the custom in Jerusalem—a correct and good custom, and it is Torah that one should not delay marriage until a late age, etc.—you should engage with the appropriate energy in searching for a good shidduch. Since this question is also connected to arranging a livelihood, and taking into account the greatest necessity of our times, especially in the Holy Land (may it be rebuilt and established), to utilize all the teaching strengths found among the circles of those who are G-d-fearing, and to utilize them to their fullest, therefore, in my opinion, you should continue to strive and increase your involvement in teaching in the purity of holiness. May the words of our Rabbeinu HaZaken, author of the Tanya and Shulchan Aruch, be fulfilled in you: that through tzedakah (which also includes spiritual tzedakah), one’s mind and heart become a thousand times more refined, such that even if the time you can dedicate to Torah study on your own is reduced, the success, understanding, and comprehension within that time will increase.

I am surprised by what you write, that you have tasted the teachings of Chabad but cannot tolerate the "great light," and that you have not "merited" to continue in the ways of Chassidus. Surely you understand, even after a brief contemplation, that what you are writing is impossible. For the Torah was not given to the ministering angels, and every part of the Torah that is revealed and publicized—once anyone becomes aware of the existence of this part of Torah, the mitzvah of Torah study applies to them regarding this part as well, for Torah study is equivalent to all the mitzvos. In the inner dimension of the Torah, which is called in the holy Zohar the "soul of the Torah," it is the soul and vitality of the study of the revealed Torah as well. As for what you write, that you have not "merited" this, this is not a matter of merit, but rather as our Sages have ruled: "If one says, 'I have labored and not found,' do not believe him." It is understood that the "leaven in the dough" hinders everyone, and therefore it is almost certain that the reason here is also as mentioned above. In such a case it is said, "There shall be no strange god within you"—this refers to the yetzer hara (Shabbos 105b). May Hashem grant you success in bringing good news regarding all of the above.

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 3290

B"H, 8 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Mr. Ben Zion, known as Dr. Dinur,
Shalom u’Bracha!

Your letter has been received, and I have just now received your book, *Bemifneh Hadoros*. Thank you for this gift.

Following our conversation, I would be pleased to receive your other printed works as well, particularly *Yisrael Bagolah* and others, and I thank you in advance.

I have not yet had the opportunity to examine the aforementioned book, but I do not wish to delay my letter, as the Festival of Matzos, the time of our freedom, is fast approaching. I wish to express to you my blessing for freedom—true freedom: freedom from spiritual anxieties and material anxieties, freedom from Mitzrayim, from limitations, and from anything that hinders the life of a complete Jew.

It is superfluous to note, especially to someone like yourself, that physical freedom is not only not an end in itself—serving merely as a preparation for the freedom of the soul, as the verse states, "When you bring the people out of Mitzrayim, you shall serve Hashem on this mountain"—but that physical freedom is not even possible unless it is bound to the freedom of the soul. Furthermore, the freedom of the soul—or, in the language of Chassidus, even the freedom of the animal soul—is not possible unless it is bound to the freedom of the divine soul.

For in the battle of the inclinations, the spirit will never truly accept the authority of the material, nor will it be subservient to it for long, nor will it reconcile with it wholeheartedly. This is not the case when the spirit overcomes the material and defeats it completely—meaning, not only out of necessity, but out of the recognition that this is how it should be, that the material should be subservient to the spirit. Only then does peace truly prevail in this "small world," the human being, since the material can be refined and clarified to become a chariot for the spirit, and not the reverse. Indeed, the path to this begins with *kabalas ol*, but its end is spiritual satisfaction and true freedom. As the teachings of Chassidus express, the divine soul is always in a state of *bitul*, and through *avodah* that begins with *kabalas ol*, a person arrives from *iskafya* to *is'hapcha*—where the evil is not merely subservient to the good, but is transformed into good, which is the true purpose of man.

I will conclude with the traditional blessing, wishing you a kosher and happy Pesach.

The second part of this letter was sent to several others.

## 377

B"H, 8 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter from the end of Adar, in which you write that you have completed your studies and are standing at a crossroads regarding your future arrangements, and you ask for my opinion on this.

Since you surely wish to have an influence in a suitable environment and enjoy working with children, you should arrange yourself in the field of education. Your current knowledge is certainly sufficient in general, and if further completion is needed, it is common in our times to pursue further training simultaneously with actual work. In truth, one matter assists the other, for there is no better study than practical experience, which reveals on the spot what requires correction and what can be improved.

Regarding your question of how to influence those outside and what material to use: in our times, when there is such great ignorance even of the basic foundations and their beginnings, and one cannot rely on the street—and often not even on the home—the study of matters brought in the Torah, Neviim, and Kesuvim, the Kitzur Shulchan Aruch, and the like, is a novelty for most of the youth. The explanation for this, and the methods to increase their desire for these studies—and especially for a way of life according to the Torah—can be found in abundance in the sichos and collected talks of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, which are already in print, as well as in the monthly publications of the Merkos L'Inyonei Chinuch, which have been appearing every month for some fourteen years, and more. The main thing in all of the above is to begin the work in practice; in the course of the work, you will see your path and your method of work more clearly, and there is nothing that can stand before the will.

With blessings for success in the above and for a kosher and happy Pesach.

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita

## 3,398

B"H, 9 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter—which lacked a return address—regarding the *tikkun* for the past: you should increase your engagement in the letters of Torah and the letters of *tefillah*. The essential point is that your learning and your *tefillah* should be with *chayus* and warmth. Specifically, in the study of *piniyus haTorah*—the *neshamta d'oraysa*—you should exert influence upon your surroundings, *midah k'neged midah*. This applies especially to your friends in your *kitah*, that they too should conduct themselves in this manner. When you speak to them with words that come from the heart—and most importantly, when your own conduct is as it should be in this regard—these things will be effective. Furthermore, for the time being, you should divert your mind and thoughts from the matter about which you wrote, and occupy your power of thought with matters of Torah and *mitzvos*.

Surely you know some chapters of *Mishnayos* by heart, and at the very least one chapter of *Tanya*. Also, undertake—*bli neder*—to recite *Tehillim* every day after *davening*. May *Hashem* grant you success in all of the above.

With blessings for a kosher and happy *Pesach* holiday,

On behalf of the Rebbe, *shlita*,
Secretary

## 3199

3199

B"H, 9 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn

Greetings and blessings,

I acknowledge receipt of your letter dated 4 Nissan.

I am surprised to hear the report that you have stopped eating breakfast. Aside from the fact that I do not understand the basis for such a practice, I also do not understand the justification for it. We have only the guidance of our holy Nasi, and the teaching of the Baal Shem Tov is already well-known regarding the verse, "If you see the donkey of one who hates you... you shall surely release it with him" — and not through fasts and self-affliction. Especially in our generation, this weak generation, when the body becomes distracted because it is hungry or the like, this first and foremost leaves an impression on one’s avodah of tefillah, the study of Torah, and so on. Even if there were some reward in it, one must clarify whether the loss does not outweigh the gain. If you will listen to my opinion, you should follow the well-known practice : it is better to eat in order to daven than to daven in order to eat.

As we approach the festival of Pesach, which is coming upon us and all of Yisrael for good, may you celebrate it in a manner of kashrus and joy, and may you be able to share good news regarding the fulfillment of your heart’s desires for the good.

With holiday blessings.

## 3300

*[11 Nissan, 5715]*

**Section 1.** Regarding the law of inserting wool threads into a linen garment: You write that you have not found anyone who provides a definitive halachic ruling on this. It is surprising that you do not mention the authorities cited in Shulchan Aruch Orach Chaim, end of Section [9]. See there in the Shulchan Aruch of the Alter Rebbe, and in the books cited in Shaarei Teshuvah, some of which do not permit it even in times of great need, due to the **decree** that one might come to insert wool threads into a linen tallis. Naturally, this does not affect the halachic discussion in your responsa, which clarify several matters regarding this topic.

**Section 12.** Regarding one who davens according to the nusach of the Arizal in a beis medrash where the congregation davens according to the Ashkenaz nusach: It was pleasant for me to see your conclusion, and may it be the will of Hashem that it be accepted in every place, that it is proper to request of the congregation, etc., and peace upon Yisrael. Sadly, there are those who label themselves "zealots," specifically regarding matters of *lav* (prohibitions), whereas in other matters they do not act accordingly. And the wise will understand.

As support for your words, it is well known what is written in Magen Avraham, beginning of Section 68, that changing the text of the davening—aside from what is mentioned in the Gemara that it is equal for everyone—has an explanation in the introduction to Shaar HaKolel, Shaar HaSiddur, by my father-in-law, the Rebbe, Rabbi Dovid Lavut. See also Responsa Meishiv Davar, Section 17, and Responsa Minchas Elazar, Part 1, where he concludes that it is permitted to change from the Ashkenaz nusach to the Sefard nusach, etc.

**Section 58.** Regarding a matter of the day: Whether it is possible to fulfill the obligation of matzah with matzah that has passed twelve months or more. In my humble opinion, it is clearly permitted: a) Because we see tangibly that it is fit for human consumption and has not changed at all. b) The custom of Yisrael (which is Torah) is also brought in the Siddur of the Alter Rebbe—that one mixes matzah on Erev Pesach for all the Shabbosos of the year until the following Pesach, and even on Shabbos HaGadol it is permitted through this *eruv*.

**Even HaEzer Part 1.** Whether there is a prohibition of *petzu'a daka* (a crushed testicle) regarding a woman. See Sefer HaChinuch, Mitzvah 559, that this only applies to males. The same is stated in Meiri to Yevamos, page 21. This is the place for me to express my astonishment at the lack of attention given to the surgery known as a prostate surgery, which in our generation is performed frequently, and often without any *she'elas chacham* (consultation with a Torah scholar), even though this is a matter of castration, as the doctors cut nerves and pathways, etc. It is possible that in consultation with expert doctors, the surgery could be performed without the aforementioned. As of now, I have not found anyone to raise this issue, and those who turned to me—whom I alerted to clarify all of the above—found it to be a great novelty, as did the rabbis whom they consulted and spoke with.

3701

B"H, 11 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the esteemed Rabbi, the Chassid, the G-d-fearing man, etc.,
Rabbi Moshe Duber, may he live and be well,

Greetings and blessings!

Your letter has been received.

As we approach the festival of Matzos, the season of our freedom, which is arriving for us and for all of Israel for good, I hereby express my blessing for a kosher and joyous holiday, and for true freedom—freedom from material worries and spiritual worries—from everything that hinders the avodah of Hashem with joy and a good heart.

May you continue this freedom and joy throughout the entire year.

I have received the two booklets regarding *she'elah* and *chatzitzah*. Thank you. (It seems one of them is the booklet you gave me previously in mimeograph form). Out of affection for *pilpul* in Torah, I have said in my haste—at least (and I believe this is not inaccurate, G-d forbid)—four questions, according to the custom of this festival:

1) Regarding what you wrote on the matter of *chatzitzah*—it is surprising that you did not touch upon the clarification of the essence of the matter: whether, when there is a *chatzitzah*, there is no *tevilah* at all (since we derive it from *morchatz* in *Eruvin* 4b, and the matter of *kartzah sefaseiha* is resolved simply).

Or, perhaps the *tevilah* exists, but it is invalid (and therefore there is a distinction between a *chatzitzah* on the majority or the minority, unlike in *tevilah*. Although it is difficult to understand from where we derive this distinction, since we derive it from *morchatz* as mentioned above).

2) Regarding what you wrote in the *Rambam*, Chapter 5—it is puzzling to me, for a minority that one is particular about **does not** constitute a *chatzitzah* [by Torah law], rather the Sages decreed it.

3) It is also surprising that you did not mention the *Responsa of the Tzemach Tzedek*, *Yoreh Deah*, section 58, from which one can learn several *halachos* regarding the matter at hand.

4) Regarding what you wrote in the note on page 15—it is difficult to accept the matter of the *chatzitzah* of air between the *tefillin shel rosh* and the head, where you suggest that the Torah was not particular about this because it is the "way of placement," etc., and if so, one should minimize this *chatzitzah* as much as possible. In my humble opinion, this does not seem correct; on the contrary, the Torah was particular that the base of the *tefillin shel rosh* should be flat (and see *Menachos* 35a, and the introduction to the *Mishnah Commentary* of the Rambam: "We have seen in the *Luchos* from Yehoshua, and see further," etc.). If so, on the contrary, the placement **must** be such that only a minority touches. Were it not for the weight of the *tefillin* that presses against the skin of the head, the contact of a flat surface against a spherical surface would be only at **one** point.

With holiday blessings.

## 3,402

11 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 5 Nissan, with the *pidyon nefesh* enclosed therein, I have read it today at the holy *tziyun* of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory. Regarding what you wrote concerning your *shiurim*—that you have no answer to give, etc.—even if we were to assume that you are correct in all the reasons you detail in your letter, it is a known ruling of our holy Torah that although "the Merciful One exempts one who is coerced," we do not say "it is considered as if he performed [the deed]." This is especially true since "love covers all transgressions," and the cunning of the *yetzer hara* is well known, as per the saying of my father-in-law, the Rebbe. Therefore, even the aforementioned reasons require scrutiny. May it be the will of Hashem that, at the very least, from here on in, you will not lack the necessary consistency.

I received your blessing with pleasure, and as per the promise of the *Ribono shel Olam*, whoever blesses will be blessed. May you and your entire family, *sheyichyu*, celebrate the upcoming *Chag HaMatzos*—which is approaching us and all of *Klal Yisrael* for good—in a manner of *kashrus* and joy, and may you bring good tidings regarding all those mentioned in your letter.

With holiday blessings.

## 3,303

B"H, 11 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn

To the G-d-fearing, illustrious, and distinguished, who engages in communal needs, Moreinu HaRav Aharon Mordechai, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

... 2) Regarding the matter of the day: organizing a gathering of teachers and, primarily, school administrators, to deliberate on the upcoming term and the new school year, preparations for registration, etc. Surely, this gathering should also be utilized to consult on possible improvements in educational methods, the curriculum, and so on. Furthermore, in an informal manner, I believe the time has come to seek ways to connect with the parents of the students as well and to influence them, too. For beyond the necessity that the spirit in the home not be contrary to the influence of the school, the matter itself is of great importance, even if it is not directly the school's responsibility.

3) Regarding what you wrote about having the youth of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad teach *limudei kodesh* in a completely secular school: it is self-evident that this should not be done within the walls of that school, as it is akin to providing a *hechsher* for the school. Consequently, there is room for concern that this would lead some of the parents who are currently hesitant to send their children there. Although in some places such things are done—even in New York—this is done with emphasis that it is not within the walls of the school nor part of its curriculum, but rather through the initiative of the students (or by stirring this initiative within them). The involvement of the administration is only that they do not prevent it, or perhaps they assist in unofficial ways with the private initiative of individual students. Surely, a synagogue or another location can be found where the aforementioned students can study *limudei kodesh*. It is understood that you have permission to publicize this opinion to all those interested, and especially to Tzeirei Agudas Chabad...

And in anticipation of the Festival of Matzos, which is approaching us and all of Israel for good, may he and his entire household, may they live, celebrate it in a kosher and joyous manner.

With holiday blessings.

## 3404

11 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter informing me of the celebration for the inauguration of the Yeshiva building: It is somewhat surprising that you were so brief. While I can certainly find a merit for you—that your work is overwhelming—it is nonetheless a lack of information for us here. It is well known that in such matters, it is impossible that over time there would not be an increase in information. In any event, I was very pleased that the event was a success. You are surely aware of the custom in these countries, that the inspiration from such celebrations should be felt throughout the entire year by meeting with the *baalei batim* and reminding them—in a diplomatic fashion—of the dedication during the celebration, etc., which motivates them to increase their support, whether in their own persons or their finances. It is surely unnecessary to remind you that it would be worthwhile to have a description of the celebration in the appropriate newspaper, so as to provide an opportunity for public expression for those who participated and to arouse "the jealousy of scribes" in others. Furthermore, this elevates the status of the Yeshiva.

Regarding the aforementioned proposal about uniforms: I do not see any benefit in this. Since in our institutions in Eretz Yisrael (may it be rebuilt speedily) and in France, etc., this is not a factor, there is actually room for concern. It is impossible to satisfy everyone, and consequently, the matter of uniforms will prevent some from visiting the schools. Moreover, there is a concern that if you introduce uniforms, it will lead to questions regarding specific matters that cannot be accepted, which will result in a rift. Therefore, the matter should be dismissed by explaining the reality: that in our institutions, such a thing does not exist and never has.

As for the method of teaching the girls—whether it must be in the same building where the boys study—that is out of the question. However, it is surprising that you cannot find another place for this, as surely the number of girls prepared to study in such institutions is not large at this time.

Regarding what you wrote... that he is waiting for an agreement from my side for him to study certain subjects in the Yeshiva of...: I find the doubt in such a matter surprising, given how much my father-in-law, the Rebbe, emphasized the magnitude of this matter. May his strength be fortified; he will surely occupy himself with this as is fitting, and Hashem will grant him success. The same applies to the continuation of your letter, where you write that the *anash* who are constantly involved in the Yeshiva need inspiration. This, too, is surprising to me. What can be added to the words of my father-in-law, the Rebbe—words that carve out flames of fire—regarding *chinuch* that is kosher and *chinuch* on the purity of holiness? This is in addition to what you have seen: that he himself acted and dedicated his time, his strength, his energy, and his most internal powers to the field of *chinuch*, not only for the older ones but also for the young boys and girls. And "things that are well-known do not require proof."

Regarding the thought of participating in the celebration of the 5th of Iyar: I have recently received a letter from Eretz Yisrael (may it be rebuilt speedily) stating that several schools there—even those of the Mizrachi—are not participating in this for various reasons. This is all the more true in your country, where one should not enter into any matters of partisanship, as I have written several times. These matters should be explained to others as well: that our purpose is to occupy ourselves with *chinuch* on the purity of holiness, and nothing more. If there is any matter that, by doing it, would distance even one boy or girl from their school, this is the opposite of the goal of this institution. Regarding the aforementioned day, there are many differing opinions, from one extreme to the other. Consequently, if you take any position whatsoever, there will be various circles of *Bnei Yisrael* who will oppose you. It is understood that it is better not to enter into the question at all and to minimize it... and to write it off entirely. If that is not possible, you will surely find the appropriate words to explain as mentioned above.

With blessings for a kosher and happy *Chag HaMatzos* and for success in all the above, in addition to success in your personal matters.

## 3305

B"H, 12 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi, the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, etc.,
Rabbi Shlomo Yosef, may he live,

Peace and blessing!

...I was pleased to hear that your book of Chassidic stories is being printed. You should certainly note the source from which you took the stories, who the narrator was, and the degree of his accuracy (this applies, of course, if the stories are Chassidic tales), or whether it is a reflection of the soul's movement, etc.

Regarding what you wrote about indices for Chassidic seforim: the foundational seforim are Tanya, Torah Or, and Likutei Torah. Indices for Torah Or and Tanya have already been printed (though not in their entirety, as there was no time to prepare them as is appropriate for printed works—the second part of the indices, which includes cross-references, comparisons, etc.). As for an index to Likutei Torah, I have one in manuscript form which will surely be printed as well; He makes everything beautiful in its time.

Regarding the Dead Sea Scrolls: I have **not** had the time to examine the dispute among the researchers on this matter, as it is known that they are divided from one extreme to the other. However, what appears to me from a **superficial** reading and seeing a few photographs is that, judging by the style of the writing regarding the use of plene and defective spelling, etc., it is the work of an *am ha'aretz* (an unlearned person), who is prone to errors in transcription due to a lack of knowledge. Therefore, extreme caution is required regarding the authority of these scrolls (and who knows the reason they were hidden).

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach holiday,

**M. Schneerson**

## 379

B.H., 12 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

...May it be the will of Hashem that the days of trust and the days of faith in Hashem and in Moshe, His servant, will empower each and every one of us to be strong in our faith in the *atpashtuta d’Moshe* (extension of Moshe) of every generation—which in our generation is my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory. And this should not be a faith that remains merely a *makif* (encompassing light) that does not touch upon practical matters at all, but rather a faith that relates to the practical necessity of defending the institutions and affairs of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, with the vigor that is required and essential. For then, surely, they will melt like wax, and the Jewish people will emerge with a high hand.

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach.

...Regarding what you noted concerning what is written in *Iggeres HaKodesh*, end of *Siman* 12, that the Jewish people are by nature compassionate and perform acts of kindness because their souls are drawn from His attributes, may He be blessed, etc.—whereas in *Tanya*, end of *Perek* 1, it is written that they come from the *nefesh habahamit*.

It is understood that both are true. From the *nefesh elokis* within a Jew, compassion and *gemilut chasadim* are natural because it is drawn from His attributes, may He be blessed, etc. And the *nefesh habahamit* is also like this by nature, and it is **possible** that in a Jew, the *nefesh habahamit* is a mixture of good and evil. One should add an explanation here based on what is explained in many places in *Chassidus*: that the *nefesh habahamit* in a Jew is compatible with and has a connection to the *nefesh elokis* in a Jew. This is why the attribute of compassion and kindness prevails within it as well, even though it is obvious that it also contains opposing attributes. For the reason that it is merely a mixture of good and evil is not sufficient to explain why the *nefesh habahamit* in Jews is compassionate and performs acts of kindness. This can be reconciled by noting that at the end of *Perek* 1, it adds the word **"in their nature"** (*betoldotam*), because the *nefesh elokis* comes afterward (*Shulchan Aruch HaRav*, second edition, end of *Siman* 4).

#### ***379***

**Regarding what you noted:** See also above, Vol. 2, Letter 426; Vol. 3, 513.

## 3,707

B"H, 13 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter from the beginning of Shevat—which was received with a delay—I have mentioned you and your entire family, may they live, at the holy resting place of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory. Surely, he will awaken Divine mercy for them regarding what they need in both material and spiritual matters.

I was pleased to read about your communal activities. As for your complaint that you do not see immediate results, it is understood that this complaint comes from the *sitra achra*, which is a master craftsman that approaches each and every person with such temptations, hoping to succeed through them. As is explained in *Hayom Yom* for the 23rd of Sivan, the test for this is that if these matters cause confusion and weaken one’s actual performance of Torah and *mitzvos*, their source is from the opposing side. Consequently, one should dismiss them entirely and increase one’s efforts in *pe'ulos* and in the study of Torah, both in public and for oneself, and so on.

We are workers of the day, and Hashem will do what is good in your affairs. You will surely see success at the appropriate time, as it is written, "He has made everything beautiful in its time." Based on the above, it is also understood that those fears you have, which you write about, are founded only in the cunning and schemes of the *yetzer hara*, which is the entirety of its existence. Therefore, when one cries out with a loud voice of noise and agitation—as is explained in *Tanya*—it will melt like wax, and you will see the truth: that Hashem watches over you, just as He does over every single Jew, with individual *hashgacha*, and consequently, your heart will be firmly trusting in Hashem.

As we approach the *Chag HaMatzos* that is coming upon us and all of Israel for good, may you and your entire family, may they live, celebrate it with *kashrus* and joy.

With holiday blessings.

## 309

B"H, 1st Day of Chol Hamoed Pesach, 5715
Brooklyn.

To the distinguished Chossid, G-d-fearing, and learned,
Rabbi Shmuel Menachem Mendel, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

... With blessings.

Due to the holiness of the festival, the Rebbe shlita has not signed personally, and I am signing on his behalf,

A. Quint
Secretary

P.S.

Regarding your observation on Tanya, Chapter 37 (48a), which states: "And every spark did not descend to this world, **even though** it is a great descent, etc.," where you find the expression **"even though"** (af) to be unclear.

The word "af" in this context is not meant in the sense of a contradiction, as in the expression "even though" (af al pi), but rather in the sense of **"furthermore"** (ve'od). This is similar to the Scriptural phrasing "And yet also this" (v'af gam zos), and the Sages' usage, such as "He, too (af hu), was among those who nullified" (Sotah 9a), or "even (af) the Jordan valley," and in many other places.

One may explain why the Alter Rebbe specifically chose the word "af" here: it is the language of the verse that is **relevant to our subject**—"I have also (af) made it" (Yeshayahu 48:13), where the interpretation is likewise "furthermore." By writing "af" precisely, he is alluding to the path of the Divine (as in "He began with 'af'"), as explained in Chassidus in Likutei Torah, beginning of Parshas Balak, and elsewhere.

#### 309

Printed in Igros Kodesh with references, etc., see 5743, and completed based on a photocopy of the letter.

**Rabbi Shmuel Menachem Mendel, may he live:** Schneerson. Additional letters to him appear above in 382, and in the notes referenced there.

## Gimmel-Tav-Yud

[1st Day of Chol Hamoed Pesach, 5715]

In response to your letter of the 7th of Nissan, in which you write that you have occasion to daven in various shuls, and there are those among them who object to your learning Mishnayos and reciting Kaddish afterward, claiming that one should not increase the number of Kaddishes. It is a great wonder that this is even a matter of complaint, for this is a well-known custom throughout all the dispersions of Yisrael.

It is obvious that this does not fall under the category of "increasing the number of Kaddishes." Our Sages have said (Sotah 49a) that it is through the *Yehei Shmei Rabba* of Aggadah (which includes the study of Mishnayos, as explained in the Poskim) that **the world endures**, and that this alone saves us and protects us in the double and redoubled darkness of the generation of the footsteps of Moshiach. You will surely explain all of the above to them...

#### ***Gimmel-Tav-Yud***

Printed in Likkutei Sichos, Vol. 26, p. 415.

**That one should not increase the number of Kaddishes:** See also above, Vol. 10, letter 3,160.

## 3111

B"H, 3rd day of Chol Hamoed Pesach, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

...Regarding what you wrote concerning the avreich Shmuel Gurary, may he live, and the reasons for his traveling here: It is somewhat puzzling, given that where they are currently located there is a yeshiva with tremendous potential. This is a mitzvah that cannot be performed by others—the proof being that others are not doing it, and even among anash, the activities are not being carried out to the necessary extent, neither in quantity nor in quality (although the pressures of the times are certainly a factor, there are other reasons as well). It is therefore understood that those who have merited this and succeeded in taking part in this holy avodah should be encouraged, strengthened, and assisted by everyone else in every possible way. Yet, in return, he explains in his letter how it is supposedly good and right for him to abandon the beautiful portion that Divine Providence has granted him—to assist in the strengthening and development of the yeshiva—and to seek "great and wondrous things" across the sea, etc., etc.

As for what you wrote, that the purpose will be fulfilled because he will be able to increase his knowledge in Chassidus, etc.—even if it were true that he could only achieve this by traveling across the sea, I still doubt (and it is a doubt bordering on certainty) which is more worthwhile, even for him: to fulfill the mission of Divine Providence by working in Yeshivas Oholei Yosef Yitzchok, or to become more proficient and learned in Nigleh and Chassidus than he is now.

Especially since our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe writes in the Iggeres HaKodesh at the beginning that through tzedakah (which also includes spiritual tzedakah), one’s mind and heart become a thousand times more refined. Thus, one hour that he learns in Australia, whether in Nigleh or Chassidus, while simultaneously engaging in the aforementioned work, will be as successful as learning for **one thousand** hours elsewhere, where that engagement would be absent. This is particularly true given that I have not yet received reports that the avreichim and bochurim in Australia are utilizing all their free time for Torah and the avodah of the heart, which is tefillah. Since there is room to increase their efforts where they are now, why should he diminish the activities in the yeshiva and thereby diminish the influence of success upon himself? To elaborate on such a simple matter is superfluous...

## 3716

B"H, 26 Nissan, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letters of the 9th, 14th, and 23rd of Nissan, along with the enclosures. You have surely received my previous letter, and you should contemplate what I wrote there regarding the absolute necessity to organize one’s activities and avodah in the proper order. As it is written, "Even if you are small in your own eyes, etc." It suffices to reflect for a brief moment on the letterhead you have printed, and on the envelopes representing the Chabad movement for the country of... — beginning from Rabbeinu HaZaken, the Baal HaTanya, the glory of the Mitteler Rebbe, his son-in-law and successor the Tzemach Tzedek, and so on. In truth, even a momentary thought regarding this matter strikes one with awe at the magnitude of the responsibility. However, our hope is that the Ribono shel Olam only demands according to one’s capacity, and consequently, the strengths are provided in advance to fulfill this role in its entirety; the matter depends solely on one’s will and choice.

It is already known what is cited in the name of the Rebbe (Rashab) in *Toras Shalom*, 19 Kislev: when one stands in one matter but thinks about another, it is not good from **all** perspectives. Therefore, the emphasis in your letters regarding the upcoming month of Elul is not good. Especially since Yidden believe in the coming of Moshiach Tzidkeinu, that he will come every single day. When he asks you what you were occupied with during the months of Nissan, Iyar, etc., will you answer him that you could not engage in the matters I spoke of because you were busy with your business, preparing to travel, etc., and consequently, you did not conduct **those** matters—which Hashgacha Pratis placed you there to oversee—as they should be?

It is understood and simple that it is not the way of Chabad and Lubavitch to preach morality. Rather, my hope is that although all the discussion and talk thus far, in my opinion, have not yet brought about a change in the order of things, eventually they will begin to take effect, and the change will begin in practical terms. If so, this is not "preaching," but rather assistance from one person to another, given with joy, so that together we may fulfill the command of our Sages: "We are workers of the day."

It goes without saying that all of the above is directed not only toward you, but also toward the rest of anash who... etc. The difference is that for you, the Ribono shel Olam has succeeded in making your material livelihood a channel for pleasant spiritual avodah, whereas for other anash, one must be a *shamash*, another a *skatavoyetz* (shoemaker), and the third a merchant in material matters, and sometimes even in physical matters, etc.

My hope is that these few lines—few in quantity—will suffice to bring about movement **in actual practice**, and not merely promises, and to begin this conduct immediately. We are all assured, each and every one of us, by the Rebbe, my father-in-law, that if one only acts in this regard, it will be with success. And for grown men, the time for **playing games** is far less than for children, since they recognize the privilege granted to them to fulfill a specific role...

With blessings to hear good news regarding all of the above.

## 317

B"H, 26 Nissan, 5715
