## 3,661

B”H, 22 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 4 Tammuz, in which you write that you have heard much regarding Chassidus Chabad and its concepts, and wish to know several details about it:

It is understood that it is difficult to explain the details of a philosophical system in a letter, especially the details of a comprehensive system such as this. You can certainly find the *kuntreisim* and *sichos* that have been printed and are available in Eretz Yisrael—may it be built and established—which contain sufficient material on all the above. You should look in particular at *Kuntres Toras HaChassidus* and *Kuntres Limud HaChassidus*. And since reading a book is not always sufficient, you should **make an effort** to meet with the *mashpi’im* of the study of the teachings of Chassidus who are in Jerusalem, in Yeshivas Tomchei Temimim in Lod, Kfar Chabad, etc., as you will certainly find with them answers to many of your questions.

Regarding your question as to whether a person needs to seek out and find someone to whose voice he will listen—and it is obvious that your question refers to listening by way of *kabalas ol* (accepting the yoke), for if it is through intellectual explanation until the listener understands, then that is not listening to the voice of another, since he is waiting until the matters are accepted **by him**—this is a simple matter, and the *Mishnah* rules: "Make for yourself a *rav*." The interpretation of this is not as some say, that it applies only to the study of Torah and its understanding (which is already included in the mitzvah of Torah study, and the term "make" is not as applicable there, as it emphasizes matters done out of necessity and *kabalas ol*). The inner intent of this is specifically in the manner mentioned above.

According to the known principles of *hora’ah* (instruction), that *ma’aseh rav* (the actual conduct of a great person) is even greater than a definitive *halachah*, we see tangibly—the strength in *Torah u’mitzvos* and *yiras Shamayim* of all those who conduct themselves in the aforementioned manner, meaning that they did not rely solely on their own intellect and understanding, which had been their final arbiter. In truth, it is also said regarding them that "a bribe blinds the eyes of the wise," and *kal v’chomer*, etc.—for this does not mean that one understands intellectually that the law is with the opposing side and acts against his intellect; rather, it means that because of the bias of his will, it misleads even the intellect until it seems to him that this is the law. And if these things are said even regarding a **minor** personal benefit that causes one to distort the judgment of **another**, how much more so regarding the body and the *nefesh habehamis* (which also includes the *yetzer hara*), for "self-love covers all transgressions." As mentioned above, *ma’aseh rav* shows that they did not falter, or when there was a situation of trial for a prolonged period. This is not the case for those who have accustomed themselves to prioritizing *na’aseh* (we will do) over *nishma* (we will hear), not only during the weeks [of the year], but throughout the entire year.

It is understood that I am greatly astonished to read in your letter the question regarding why hundreds of the Children of Israel are casting off the yoke of the Kingdom of Heaven from their necks, etc. This is the very argument of the *mesisim* (inciters) and *medichim* (those who lead astray), and with this argument they approached the first Hebrew, our forefather Avraham. As it is stated in the *Midrash*, he was called "Avraham the Hebrew" because he was on one side and the entire world was on the other side, even though Adam HaRishon, Chanoch, Mesushelach, etc., were perfectly righteous and had many students—which was not the case in the days of Avraham. And yet, it was already said: "**One** was Avraham, and he inherited the land." Especially since anyone who studies—even superficially—the *beraisa* at the end of *Sotah*, will find the entire situation of our days explained there in all its details.

The principle of our Sages is already known in this and similar matters: "Man looks at the eyes, but Hashem looks at the heart." Let each person view himself as balanced, and the entire world as balanced, and in every action of his—even one that seems to him the most minor—he can tip the scale for himself and for the entire world toward the side of merit. Then, immediately, Israel is redeemed, the glory of Hashem is revealed, and "all flesh shall see," etc. All the questions one has regarding this are the craft of the *yetzer hara*, which clothes itself in a question and dresses it in words of intellect that have a veneer of holiness. And if, Heaven forbid, one listens to it, then today it tells him this, etc., until it tells him, "Go and worship idolatry."

May Hashem grant you success, as soon as possible, to set a fixed time for the study of *Pnimiyus HaTorah* in a way that is *aliba d’nafshi* (personally internalized), with the prerequisite for absorbing the holy Torah: prioritizing *na’aseh* over *nishma*. Then you will be happy, and not only in spirituality, but also in physicality.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

B"H, 22 Tammuz, 5715

However, he is unique in this among all the counters of the mitzvos, according to my search until now. Therefore, it must be that the other counters of the mitzvos interpret the aforementioned Gemara as being an *asmachta*, an *avizaryehu*, or the like.

## 3663

B"H, 23 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn

Greetings and blessings!

Following your long silence, your letter of 17 Tammuz has arrived. Regarding what you wrote concerning the various prizes—for those who are *matmidim*, for those who attend *davening*, etc.:

One should be aware of the customs in other *yeshivos* regarding this. It is understood that one must be careful not to create a situation where it is said that we have moved from a "greater holiness to a lesser holiness." This also applies to the vacation times in the *yeshiva*. In truth, the concern that there should be "no less than in other *yeshivos*" is misplaced, for ultimately, it must be evident that... this is a Chassidic *yeshiva*...

Regarding what you wrote about so-and-so who is suggesting that he study the book *Etz Chaim*: While it is understood that one who studies this is indeed studying the Torah of the Living G-d—the inner dimension of Torah—nevertheless, when these matters are studied within the books of Chassidus Chabad, they are understood correctly, and interpretations that are not as they should be—*chas v'shalom*—are refuted. This is not the case when one studies without the aforementioned explanations, etc., which requires caution. This is similar to what you also quote in your letter from *Shoresh Mitzvas HaTefillah*, Chapter 2, in the name of the Baal Shem Tov (and with this, his question is also answered—how to interpret the aforementioned words of the Baal Shem Tov in conjunction with what is explained in many places regarding the necessity of studying the teachings of Chassidus). You should have explained all this to him in terms appropriate for him and helped him understand well that since he is involved in the study of the holy *Zohar*, etc., it is **even more necessary** for him to study the teachings of Chabad, in accordance with the aforementioned words of the Baal Shem Tov.

Your observation regarding the text in *Shoresh Mitzvas HaTefillah*, Chapter 7, concerning the phrase "the attribute of mercy, roll upon us," is surprising, as this has already been publicized in many places, including at the end of *Kuntres 115*, page 48, in the note there. In the books cited there, you will find an answer to your difficulties regarding this.

Regarding what you wrote about the *Sulam* commentary on the *Zohar*: I have not seen it, other than glancing at it for a few moments, and it is understood that one cannot express an opinion based on that. The book is not in my possession. From what I hear, he has paved a path for himself in the study of *Etz Chaim* and the *Zohar*, but we have only the "king's highway" of our *Nasi* in the teachings of Chassidus...

Regarding your question about the study of the inner dimension of Torah: In *Hilchos Talmud Torah*, the Alter Rebbe (beginning of Chapter 2) counts it as part of the "third" of *Talmud*, while in *Likutei Torah*, *Vayikra* (5c), it is counted as part of the "third" of *Mikra*. This depends on which part of the inner dimension of Torah one is studying—whether it is an explanation of the verses or a *shakla v'tarya* (analytical discussion) in understanding and conceptualization. Look closely and you will find the distinction in the laws regarding this in *Likutei Torah*, the Rambam, and the *Shach* (the sources in *Shulchan Aruch* there). From this, it is understood that it is also possible for the study of the inner dimension of Torah to be like the study of the *halachos pesukos* (definitive laws) in the *Mishnah* and *Talmud*, which is in the world of *Yetzirah* (the "third" of *Mishnah*). This explains what is written in *Pri Etz Chaim*, *Shaar Hanhagas HaLimud* (84b), and in many places, that *Midrash* and *Aggadah* are in *Yetzirah*. Yet, it is written in *Likutei Torah* there that the inner dimension of Torah is learned from "even if I have made them" (*af asisiv*)—*Mikra*—because this is the essence and the root. Just as *Talmud* is in *Beriyah*, even though the *halachos pesukos* within it are in *Yetzirah*, and so on.

## 3764

B"H, 23 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn.

To the Administration of Yeshivas Tomchei Temimim in Sifria – Kfar Chabad,
May they live,

Peace and blessing!

...I was pleased to hear that more students from the Reshet schools have been accepted, and you should certainly continue to exert yourselves in this with added vigor. It would be appropriate to establish a permanent class for this type of student, for several reasons...

If such conduct is worthwhile in all cities and settlements, how much more so in a settlement of Anash.

With blessings for good tidings in all the above.

## 3765

B"H, 23 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 17 Tammuz, in which you write regarding the state of your health and that in the past your pregnancies have, G-d forbid, not come to term.

In matters such as these, one must strengthen one's trust in the **private** Divine Providence over every single Jew. It is the ultimate good, and He will surely bestow upon you kindness and goodness in a revealed and manifest way. The stronger the trust, and together with this, the more you are careful to observe—as is the custom of virtuous Jewish women—the precise laws of Taharas HaMishpacha, the laws of modesty, and the conduct of your home in general, the more quickly and abundantly the blessings you require will be drawn down, especially in the matter of children. And since in all matters one must also act through natural means, you should consult an expert physician regarding appropriate conduct during the days of pregnancy. You should certainly refrain from lifting heavy loads, excessive running, and the like. And when you conceive, in a good and auspicious hour, do not publicize this, except to your closest relatives, until you enter the fifth month of your pregnancy.

With blessings for good news regarding all of the above.

P.S.
If the mezuzos in your home have not been checked within the last twelve months, it is advisable to have them checked now.

**Do not publicize:** See also above, Vol. 8, Letter 2636, and the references in the notes there.

## 3668

B”H, 24 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 20 Tammuz, in which you write regarding the trait of laziness and ask for advice on how to overcome it: You should study with concentration the beginning of Chapter 41 of Tanya, at least until the words "before the King" (there, page 2), and memorize it. From time to time, review it, whether by speaking the words or at least by contemplating them. When you contemplate even the simple explanation of these lines, it will suffice to banish laziness entirely.

Regarding what you wrote—that some say you take only the external aspect of Chassidus and not the internal—it is understood that even this is better than not taking the external aspect at all. This is analogous to what is stated in Maseches Menachos, Chapter 8, Mishnah 5: "The third of the first is equal to the first of the third." Especially since you recognize that you have not yet grasped the internal aspect, you will certainly not rest or be still; you will exert yourself, with both the exertion of the soul and the exertion of the body, to touch upon the internal aspect as well, and subsequently, until it eventually becomes more than just a superficial touch. The advice for this is to study **at length** and to conduct yourself according to the guidance of the teachings of Chassidus, without waiting until you can explain it to yourself or until you have a solid grasp (*aplayg*) of it.

It is surprising that you ask whether you should study Tanya with avreichim and bochurim. If you have the opportunity to spread the wellsprings outward, what is the doubt? You must be diligent in this, as our Sages said, "Grab and eat, grab and drink; if not now, when?" And if regarding material charity it is said that one’s mind and heart become refined, how much more so is this true when one gives charity that is both material and spiritual together.

With blessings for good tidings in all the above,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

P.S. It is surprising that you do not mention the farbrengens of the days of the Chag HaGeulah, 12 and 13 Tammuz—our redemption and the salvation of our souls—and what you gained from them in the days following the farbrengen. Surely you will complete this as soon as possible at the next opportunity.

## 3771

B"H, 25 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 24 Tammuz, in which you mention that you see a change this year regarding your trip to the country.

There is room to say that this is due to the beginning of the gathering [of...] in which Divine Providence also privileged you with the mission to organize the matter, and after a **few** days you left it in the middle (perhaps it should say: at its beginning) in order to hasten the trip to the country. It is understood that this is not the way—to prioritize the health of the body at the expense of the health of the soul. This is especially true after I told you when you were in my room that one should not be impressed by the fact that there will not be a large number of participants, since this is the first time and it will serve as a beginning. From this itself, it is also understood that this is what the statement of our Nasi refers to: "As one sets oneself up, so it goes." If, after two or three days, it occurred to you that it is better to go to the mountains than to occupy yourself with **this business**, what will happen after the beginning, when the matter becomes "old"?

It is understood and simple that the excuse for all of this is **readily available**—that so-and-so did not help them, and so-and-so did not assist them, and such-and-such a matter was not ready, etc. But when do such excuses apply? They are appropriate when the matter at hand is something one is obligated to fulfill, and consequently, a loud announcement is made, "Look at what has come!" If there was not a large crowd at the time to applaud and say "May your strength be firm," etc., then one has already done what was incumbent upon him, and no person has a claim against him. But if one looks at the matter as it truly is—namely, that this is a matter of studying the revealed Torah and the teachings of Chassidus, where not only are "all the worlds nullified before one detail of Torah" (and it is understood that the United States and its affairs are included in the general category of "the world"), but even heavenly objects cannot compare to it—then one would not have been in such great haste to "finish" the matter. Consequently, the Yetzer Hara would not have invented excuses and pretexts, and consequently, the matter of the country would also have come about in its proper time and season, rectified both in the eyes of the body and in the eyes of the soul.

It is understood and simple that it is not my role to be a moralist (and the proof is that I did not stir him regarding all of the above even after I became aware of this regarding the gathering and its "duration"). However, since you mentioned the matter of the country in your letter, I am compelled to reveal my perspective as I see the matter, and I ask for your forgiveness.

It is also understood and simple that since the damage has already been done, and one does not cry over the past, may it be the will of Hashem that, at the very least, from here on out, your stay and that of your family—may they live—in the country will be successful, both physically and spiritually, and that you will mention everyone at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

**The statement of our Nasi:** See also above, letter 3736, and the references in the notes there.

## 3772

B"H, 25 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

Following the long hiatus, I received your letter of 15 Tammuz, in which you write regarding the education in the [immigrant] transit camp, where you began visiting the schools, after which they prevented you from doing so. You wrote letters to various offices requesting permission to visit the schools, but the letters have borne no fruit.

It is understood that in a vital matter such as the education of the younger generation—as our Sages of blessed memory said, "If there are no kids, there will be no goats"—the aforementioned approach is not sufficient in the slightest. Even if we were to assume that they would permit you to visit the schools and even to examine the students, it is obvious that you would have no influence in the school whatsoever. Consequently, this does not touch upon the yiras shamayim and the observance of mitzvos of the students, which is, after all, the ultimate goal of education.

Surely, in the transit camp, there are people who desire an appropriate education for their children. Furthermore, there are several parties in Eretz Yisrael (may it be rebuilt and established) that, for various reasons, are interested in this. Surely these religious parties also have members in the aforementioned camp. Therefore, it is entirely incomprehensible why, throughout all this time, it has not been possible to organize extra hours or classes for the children in the shul or another suitable location, where they could be taught the halachos necessary for their daily lives, matters of yiras shamayim in general, and the like. Once there is a beginning, even on a small scale, surely more boys and girls—of whom you are currently unaware—will join. As is known from the saying of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory: when you place a lantern in the darkness of the night, people gather around it. This matter does not involve expenses, at least in the initial period, since one can find volunteers among the residents and those serving in holy work to engage in this, at least until the project expands. The situation in the camp is similar to that in many places in Eretz Yisrael (may it be rebuilt and established), and you can consult with suitable people in those other places to devise a plan. It has already been said, "In the multitude of counselors there is salvation," and it is known that one action is better than a thousand sighs.

With blessings for success in all the above and for good tidings.

## 3773

B"H, 25 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn

To the esteemed Rabbi, G-d-fearing, learned, and distinguished, R. Avraham HaLevi,

Shalom u’Bracha,

In response to your letter of 3 Tammuz, in which you write that you have sent several booklets of your work, "Nasiv Yashar," and are requesting an approbation (haskamah).

It is already known that, for several reasons, it is not my custom to provide approbations for books. May the Ribono shel Olam grant you success in magnifying Torah and glorifying it—a Torah that leads to action, rooted in yirah of Hashem and ahavah of Hashem. As stated in the Zohar and Tikkunei Zohar, it is specifically through ahavah and yirah that [the Torah] ascends on high. This is also a known teaching of our Rebbe, the Alter Rebbe (the author of Tanya—the authority on the hidden aspects of Torah—and the Shulchan Aruch—the authority on the revealed aspects of Torah), and it is hinted at in the Gemara, Pesachim 50b.

As is my custom, I have provided some notes on your booklets in the margins.

With blessings for success in all of the above.

***

**Notes and references to Chazal:** "Be a questioner..." and "consequently the halachic discussion expands..." and "Do I not know that I am speaking correctly?" (Bava Metzia 84a). And this is so, even though one does not raise questions on a drush.

**Booklet 1, at the beginning:** Regarding the statement that grasses were not explicitly commanded regarding kilayim (hybridization). Based on the Yerushalmi, Kilayim 1:7, it is understood simply that there is no prohibition, or at the very least, no obligation in this regard (see the references in Chullin 60a in the Maharsha there, Torah Sheleimah, and elsewhere).

**Booklet 2, at the beginning:** "From a thread to a shoe strap." A shoe strap is also property, not a person. Simply put, it can be explained: "From a thread"—which is among the ornaments and garments—of the head (Shabbos 6:1), "to a shoe strap"—which pertains to the foot.

**Booklet 3, at the beginning:** Be'er Sheva. According to the **peshat**, the names in Bereishis 21:31 and 21:33 are distinct, as explained by the Sforno.

**Booklet 4, at the beginning:** "The camel is Kelipas Nogah." See Meorei Or, entry "Chazir," that it is Kelipas Nogah (and therefore only the pig is destined to be returned to us, unlike the camel, the rock badger, and the hare. The pig is unique in its signs, whereas the camel is equivalent to the rock badger and the hare. And in Parshas Re'eh, it is mentioned in its own verse). Regarding the omission of camels, it can be explained: 1) According to the peshat, Yaakov’s messengers rode on camels—as Chazal state in Bereishis Rabbah (ch. 50:14) on the verse "and they rode upon the camels"—and therefore it was not necessary to mention them. Even so, he mentioned a "servant," for a messenger can be his son or the like.

2) According to the drash—and Chazal in Bereishis Rabbah state that the camel hints at its sign of impurity regarding Esav, who is impure—while Yaakov is pure. Therefore, he did not mention it (this is further clarified according to what is explained in Torah Or by the Alter Rebbe, beginning of Parshas Vayishlach, that Yaakov thought that Esav had already been purified. See there).

**Booklet 5, at the beginning:** Regarding the explanation of "lemor" (to say) as being imprecise. According to the peshat, on the contrary, the addition of **lemor** indicates a complete clarification and precision (see Ramban, Shemos 6:10). Here, too, it should be explained this way: that I did not merely hear a report, but [I heard it] also "lemor"—with complete clarity.

***

**3773**

**R. Avraham:** Horowitz, may his city be built and established.

## 3114

B”H, 25 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 18 Tammuz, in which you write about your attendance at the shul and beis medrash of the Chabad Chassidim in Meah Shearim, and regarding your shiurim in Torah.

It is surely unnecessary to remind you of the words of our Sages, "Whoever adds, they add to him," and as is well known regarding the virtue of Bnei Yisrael, who are called "those who walk" (*mehalchim*) because they go from level to level. As explained in Chassidus, this is the entire purpose of the soul’s descent from a high roof to a deep pit—that specifically through this, it is given the ability to ascend with an incomparable elevation. From this itself, it is understood that if, G-d forbid, one does not utilize the time and the ability for this ascent, it is not merely a prevention of gain and virtue; rather, the descent becomes a descent without purpose, and consequently, a fall, G-d forbid, from the high roof to the deep pit, as explained at length in one of the general letters of last year. Since the Ribono shel Olam does not demand more than one’s capacity, the possibility is given to each and every person in his own standing and situation to achieve this ascent. It is already known what is said, "Little by little I will drive them out," and therefore one should not be discouraged if, as is the way of most of Yisrael, things proceed little by little for you as well. On the contrary, with such a progression, the conquest is more enduring...

Regarding what you wrote concerning the names of a groom and his father-in-law, and whether one must be careful about this: generally, one should ask a chacham, especially when the names are identical. However, in your case, where the name of one is... and the name of the second is...

The Tzemach Tzedek has already written (Responsa Even HaEzer, end of Part I) that there is no room for concern at all, see there.

Regarding what you noted about the passage in the sefer Derech Mitzvotecha, Mitzvas Milah, citing the writings of the Arizal, which states: "And all *Chesed* is the gematria of *Havayah* etc., and two *Chasadim* and two-thirds are 70," and you point out that the calculation is not precise.

These matters are explained in many places, among them Shaar HaMitzvos, Parshas Lech Lecha, Etz Chaim, Shaar 33 at the beginning, and elsewhere. It is possible that these seforim are not available to you, and therefore I will transcribe the relevant passage for your question. This is the text in Shaar HaMitzvos:

"And behold, the calculation of 26 cannot be divided into three equal parts etc., and therefore it is necessary that the first part should be joined to its fellow etc., taking the remainder. And behold, the correct reason is that the majority takes the surplus, and they are the two," and the matter is explained there according to the teachings of sod, as well as the reality of taking the remainder in a different manner and its concept.

With blessings.

## 3756

B"H, 27 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn

To the esteemed and distinguished, etc., R' Yona, may he live:

Greetings and blessings!

I acknowledge receipt of your letter dated the 24th of Tammuz. I am surprised that you do not mention at all how you utilized the days of the 12th and 13th of Tammuz—the days of redemption of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, our redemption and the liberation of our souls. If, G-d forbid, this time was not utilized to hold a *farbrengen* and to arouse [people] toward Torah, *mitzvos*, and good resolutions, then, according to the words of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, that "one good deed is more effective than a thousand sighs," I do not wish to use my letter to sigh over the past. Rather, I hope that you will energetically seek an opportunity to gather with those who are connected to this (for in truth, all Jews are connected to Torah and *mitzvos* without distinction, because regarding every single one of them, the statement which is the foundation of the entire Torah was said: "I am Hashem your G-d," in the singular form). The intent of this gathering should be to bring about *hisorerus* and resolutions for the future in matters of *yiras shamayim*, Torah, and *mitzvos*. One should not think that he is "one in a field" [alone], for everyone is a *shaliach* of Hashem, the Creator and Guide of the world. He, blessed be He, is the Master of the house at every time and in every place, even in Bogota during the time of *galus*, no less than in the Holy of Holies during the time when the Beis Hamikdash was standing. There is no need to elaborate on such a simple matter.

Regarding the question asked in the Maharsha (*Shabbos* 105a), who says that the letter *tav* indicates the plural, and [the difficulty is that] *nimretzes*—with a *tav*—is in the singular form: This is not difficult. The Maharsha does not say that *nimretzes* here is a plural form; rather, he says that the *tav* indicates the plural. His intent is that in the verse, it could have been written *nimretzah* or *nimretzes*, and the fact that it was chosen specifically with the letter *tav*—which, according to grammar, is a sign and indicator of the plural—is to hint that within the single curse, many [curses] were included.

This is even more understandable when taking into account that: a) Our Sages interpret verses in many places by changing the vowels (*al tikrei banayich ela bonayich*, and others). b) In the plural, the ending is *os* (vav-tav), yet hundreds of times in *Tanach* it appears without a *vav* as well (*avos*, *avos*, etc.). If in the aforementioned case it had been written *nimretzah*, there would be no possibility of interpreting it as a plural form. However, by writing *nimretzes*, the possibility is provided to read and interpret it as *nimretzos* (with a *cholam*), as a plural form.

With blessings for success in all of the above and for good tidings regarding matters of physical livelihood.

## 3777

B.H., 28 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn

Greetings and blessings!

I acknowledge receipt of your letter from 24 Tammuz. It is surprising that there was such a long gap between your letters, and also that you do not mention in this letter anything regarding your participation in the farbrengens of the days of our redemption, 12 and 13 Tammuz—the redemption of our souls and our liberation—in which you surely participated. For it is known that a Chassidishe farbrengen can accomplish what even the angel Michael cannot, as is brought in the sichos of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory—and it has already been printed in the *Kuntres Tehillim* and elsewhere.

Thank you for what you wrote regarding the dissolution of the United Front. It is unnecessary to elaborate on the great sorrow and damage caused by its dissolution. You wrote correctly that my desire and aspiration was to establish it, and I believe I did everything within my power to do so in ways of pleasantness—for several reasons, I did not want to pursue other methods. However, regarding what you write—that my aspiration was for a united religious front **at least** for the elections—I have emphasized many times that my opinion is not as you say, but **only and exclusively** for the sake of the elections. For as great as the benefit of establishing this religious front is regarding the elections, the damage of a non-technical bloc—a bloc of ideologies—is even greater. To our sorrow and regret, we have seen tangibly that in such a merger, the *tachton* (the lower force) prevails. When we spoke about the merger of the four parties, there was a great and near-certain fear that this *tachton* would mean the *tachton* of the *tachton*, as their ideologies would prevail in this merger. The decline in the ideologies of all the parties compared to their ideologies of ten years ago is sufficient, even in their own estimation.

These words apply to all religious parties, and all the more so regarding a merger of parties.

One of the questions that was immediately on the agenda after the elections is the question of joining the coalition. My assessment is certain: had there been a merger or a religious front—not merely a technical one—they would certainly have decided to participate in the coalition, whereas in my opinion, it is forbidden according to Torah. The distinction is simple: in participating in the elections—by the one who received an identity card, etc., meaning he recognizes the offices—by his participation in the elections, he provides additional strength to the religious situation and does not bear responsibility for the laws and decrees, etc., for it is in his power to protest with all force against even a slight infringement upon the religion of Israel, and even a custom, etc. This is not the case when one enters the coalition, for then he bears responsibility—through his participation—for all the laws and decrees, etc. There is no need to elaborate on this simple matter.

Regarding what you wrote—that you did not receive a response to several of your letters—those letters did not contain matters requiring a response, but rather requests to mention family members for what they needed. I fulfilled the request and mentioned them at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory.

It is surprising that once again the shipments of printed materials from the places where your influence reaches have ceased. The reason is not understood, and you will surely clarify it.

With blessings for good tidings in personal and communal matters.

3778

## 3199

B"H, 28 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 25 Tammuz, along with the enclosed letter, which were evidently sent to explain the reason for your agitation upon receiving this correspondence. The reason, it seems, is that I did not personally sign it, and perhaps you are reading various interpretations into this. There is no need to do so, as there are no ulterior motives, Heaven forbid. The reason I did not sign it myself—even though, **naturally**, I read all letters addressed to me and also **dictate the wording of the response**—is that regarding the signature, I only sign personally when the content of the letter requires it; that is, when it contains matters of personal spiritual requests or specific guidance in Nigleh or Chassidus. This is unlike the aforementioned letter, the primary purpose of which was to acknowledge receipt of your letter and to draw your attention to the content of my letter to the youth of Agudas Chasidei Chabad, which I did sign. Since the conclusion of my letter... is that it pertains to each and every one of us, it is as if—and perhaps even without the "as if"—it was written to you personally, and with my signature.

Because of the sheer simplicity of the matter, it did not even cross my mind to provide assurances (ba'varenen) against other interpretations of the situation. However, since I see in your letter—and even more so between the lines—that there are matters confusing you, I have written these lines. May Hashem help you strengthen Yiddishkeit and disseminate it wherever you are, and to do so with proper health and peace of mind.

With blessings.

## 3880

3880

By the Grace of G-d
29 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the Administration of the Vocational School in Kfar Chabad
May they live

Peace and Blessing!

I was alarmed to receive information from an outside source that the students are being given, or have already been given, a vacation starting from the beginning of the month of Menachem Av, and perhaps even earlier, and that they subsequently intend to grant a vacation at the end of Elul and the beginning of Tishrei, etc., etc. It is astonishing that such a thought could even be entertained; in a situation like this, it constitutes a staggering *bitul Torah*.

This is especially true during the days when there are no secular studies due to necessity—days that should, in any event, be utilized for the students of the vocational school to make up the hours missed in *limudei kodesh* throughout the year. And if, for some reason or ulterior motive, such a thought arose, why was consent not requested from here? Since I am a participant in the administration of the vocational school, I also bear responsibility for this *bitul Torah* of several dozen children of Israel who have already reached the age of *mitzvos*. Furthermore, they rely on the fact that since the institution belongs to me, it is surely with my knowledge and consent—and perhaps even more than that—they tell them that for several months of the year they are exempt from studying Torah, and therefore it is certainly correct and there is no room for doubt.

In particular, beyond all of the above, the very presence of the students in the vocational school also provides supervision regarding matters of *yiras shamayim* and the performance of *mitzvos maasiyos*, and closing the vocational school also cancels this supervision. I do not know who has the broad shoulders to take this upon themselves.

Regarding the teaching of the trade—which is connected to outside offices—I do not know the arrangements in this regard, nor to what extent one can influence them. However, regarding *limudei kodesh*, the vocational school must remain open throughout the entire year. Only for specific students should a vacation of a few days be granted, in order to see their parents or for a break for rest between one study period and the next, but not for all students at once, and not during those days when it is most critical that the students be within the walls of the *beis medrash* or the *shul*, and not outside of them.

I do not know the arrangements regarding the salaries, but if, for the summer months, they do not pay in schools that grant a vacation, it is understood that I will endeavor to provide the salary for these months according to the aforementioned conditions. It is within your authority, your ability, and your responsibility to convey my proposal, request, and demand to all the *limudei kodesh* teachers at the aforementioned school, and my hope is strong that they will fulfill it. If the teachers themselves require a rest of a few weeks for reasons of health or other reasons, they should arrange it so that it is not for everyone at once, and consequently, the vocational school will remain open the entire time.

It is also understood and simple that if there are such students who will under no circumstances agree to study the entire year without a break other than for a few days, they should not be expelled from the vocational school for this. However, my hope is that their number will be very small, and will decrease even further when they see that the arrangements at the vocational school, even at the end of the summer, are good, both materially and spiritually.

I am sending this letter urgently, and you will surely inform me in return regarding all of the above, and it would be best if possible by telegram.

With blessings for good tidings and success,

**M. Schneerson**

## 3781

B.H., 29 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn

To the esteemed Rabbi, G-d-fearing, learned, and diligent, a man of many accomplishments,
Rabbi Avraham Aharon, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter, together with the book, *Sefer Chassidim* with your commentary, *Mishnas Avraham*. Although time has not permitted me to study it as thoroughly as it deserves, out of appreciation for the work and as a token of thanks and esteem, I have looked through its pages, and as a sign of this, I have provided some cursory notes, as mentioned above.

Many thanks for this gift. I would be doubly grateful if you would instruct those who carry out your wishes to send me your other books as well.

In a separate package, I am sending you some of the books published by our publishing house, which I am sure will interest you and are consistent with the high quality of your own work.

With respect and blessings.

**General Notes:**

**1.** Our generation, unfortunately, is not like the generations of old, which enjoyed relative tranquility, free from constant displacement and the like. Today, Torah and its books occupy the most important place in every home (even among those who do not understand the text), and all other matters are willingly and gladly set aside for them.

Therefore, in my opinion, it is a mitzvah and a **duty** for every publisher of books on Torah, mitzvos, yiras shamayim, and the like, to print them in a format that is convenient for use—easy to carry from house to house, to a small library, etc. Any deficiency in the convenience of using a book is liable to prevent many people from purchasing it and studying or reading it. We must not give up on these people either, as it is written: "For you do not know which will succeed," and perhaps they will produce worthy offspring. While I commend your efforts regarding the high quality of your publication (the internal quality—the indexing, etc., and the external quality—the type, paper, etc.), the format of the book is in great contradiction to the aforementioned. I have seen this in practice as well, in the distribution of books published by Kehot in both large and small formats.

**2.** The *Bologna* edition of *Sefer Chassidim* was published in Eretz Yisrael and was accepted and sanctified by the authorities of the past and present. It is simple that in any contradiction or variation, one must decide—in practice—according to this edition, and not according to other manuscripts, which should only be used to clarify matters and the like. This is especially true given the reasoning that the Bologna edition is the final one.

Therefore, any publication of *Sefer Chassidim* whose goal is also practical halacha must be based specifically on the Bologna edition (restoring the censored omissions, etc.), while it would be of great benefit to include in such an edition the variations from other manuscripts, to explain them, etc.

***

**Tzava’ah, Section 1 (in your edition):** See also the ruling of the Tzemach Tzedek in *Shulchan Aruch Yoreh Deah*, sec. 116. *Responsa Tzafnas Paneach* by the Gaon of Rogatchov, sec. 128.

Regarding the names of the mothers of the *chosson* and *kallah* if one is particular about their equivalence, see *Sdei Chemed*, entry *Chosson v'Kallah*, subsection 10.

**In the book, Section 1:** You wrote at length in your commentary regarding the essence of a *chassid*—[in the Mekitzei Nirdamim edition, in the notes to sec. 875, he cites various sources in the Shas and Rishonim where the title *chassid* is mentioned. See also *Shaarei Kedushah* by R. Chaim Vital, end of Part 1, regarding this]—and in my opinion, this is unnecessary here (unlike in sec. 875 and thereafter), because the author did not come here **at all** to define the essence of a *chassid*. Consequently, he uses the term as a borrowed name, including all those who fear G-d, even those who are not *chassidim* according to the **precise** definition of this title. For he is addressing **all** who fear G-d and think of His name in these words, and he is writing the book for all of them.

What is written, "a *chassid* whose heart goes..." is not at all intended to explain the essence of a *chassid* and *chassidus*. The presentation of the matter is: there is a *chassid* who is great in Torah and deeds, and there is a *chassid* who has the desire for this but has not received it from his teacher and has not understood it on his own; consequently, he is also lacking in deed, and one is punished for this as well. "Therefore I said I will write a book" for them.

**Section 310:** Regarding the reading of the Torah, see *Tzafnas Paneach* on the Rambam, *Hilchos Tefillah*, ch. 12.

**Section 549:** Regarding the presence of "He" and "Hidden" in the text of the blessings, see *Zohar* Vol. 3, 289a; *Tikkunei Zohar* 439; *Sefer HaBahir* 183-184; and in the book noted in the glosses of R. Avraham Margaliyos there. One should add to them and to yours: *Shibolei HaLeket HaShalem*, sec. 165; *Pri Etz Chaim*, Gate 2, end of ch. 2; *Likutei Torah* by our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe, *Parshas Masei* (91b).

**At the end of the book, sec. 600:** The sin involved here can be explained simply as moving *muktzah* (and eating it) and untying a knot. I later saw in the glosses of *Mishnas Chassidim* that he points to *Magen Avraham*, sec. 338, and *Pri Megadim* there. See also *Shulchan Aruch* of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe there, subsection 18.

#### 3781

**Rabbi Avraham Aharon:** Preis.

## 3822

B"H, 29 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn

To the illustrious Rabbi, the esteemed Chassid, engaged in communal needs, descendant of holy lineage, etc.,
Rabbi Yechezkel Shraga, shlita,

Peace and blessing!

In response to your letter of 3 Sivan, accompanied by four pages from your book *Divrei Yechezkel Shraga* which is currently being printed, and concluding with a request for a haskamah (approbation).

Regarding this, I have followed the path and custom as I saw from my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, who surely conducted himself in this matter—as in all his holy conduct—according to the custom of his father, the Rebbe Rashab, of blessed memory: not to provide haskamos for books. And "the matter is precious to those who possess it."

May it be the will of Hashem that you increase your strength for Torah, with breadth of mind, and may you succeed in strengthening pure yiras shamayim and disseminating the study of the inner dimension of the Torah—which is called in the holy Zohar "the Tree of Life" by the Ra'aya Mehemna, etc. As explained in the Tanya of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe, in *Iggeres HaKodesh*, ch. 26, this is when the statement of our Sages regarding Torah study in general is fulfilled: "it becomes for him a potion of life." This is explained at length in the *Kuntres Eitz Chaim* of the Rebbe Rashab, and it is understood through the awesome and wondrous things written at length by Rabbi Chaim Vital in his introduction to *Shaar HaHakdamos*.

With respect and blessing.

For the sake of the matter, I have added here some notes regarding the aforementioned booklets, even though this is only a cursory glance, due to my many preoccupations.

**Regarding Letter 1.** Dveikus in the Tzaddik, and at times to a very great degree—this is understood in the best way according to what is written in the holy Tanya of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe, ch. 2. From there it is understood that it can reach the point of "becoming one," like a limb that acts according to the instructions of the head and brain, whatever they may be. One may also note the *Mechilta* on the verse, "And they believed in Hashem and in Moshe His servant."

It is surprising that you were brief in this letter regarding the necessity of studying the inner dimension of the Torah, which in recent generations has been elucidated in the teachings of the Baal Shem Tov, his students, and his students' students. The absence of this study, Heaven forbid—or even studying it with less than one's capacity—hinders the Geulah and prolongs the Golus, etc., etc. As Rabbi Chaim Vital writes at length (ibid.), anyone who contemplates his words for himself will have the hair of his head stand on end. And since, from the days of the students of the Baal Shem Tov, we have also merited the revelation of the light of Torah that provides understanding and comprehension—at the very least through parables, etc.—in the study of the inner dimension of Torah, the law regarding other parts of the Oral Torah applies here as well: that one who studies but does not understand is not considered to be studying at all (*Magen Avraham*, end of ch. 50; see the *Shulchan Aruch* of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe there; and in his *Likutei Torah*, Vayikra 5b). Even if one is coerced by a lack of knowledge, or does not yet know of the existence of the study of the inner dimension of Torah, one receives reward even for reciting the letters and the like (see *Pardes*, ch. 1, and elsewhere)—even though this ignorance is a sin for which one is punished, as it is written, "I have sinned because I did not know" (see *Shelah*, end of parshas Balak). And [regarding] one who says, "The entire Torah was given to Moshe at Sinai, except for..."

It is already known what the Mashiach answered the Baal Shem Tov: that he will come when "your fountains spread outward"—spread, specifically. From this, the magnitude of the duty and responsibility of everyone whom Hashem has graced with influence over others is understood and simple: to bring those they influence to these fountains; and "from the general [affirmation] one understands the specific [negation]." And whoever does not have the Beis HaMikdash built in his days, Heaven forbid, etc. May Hashem merit us to the fulfillment of the prophecy of immersion in these fountains, as hinted at also in the Rambam, end of *Hilchos Mikvaos*: "All will know Me... for the earth will be filled with the knowledge of Hashem as the waters cover the sea."

**Regarding Letters 2-3.** Regarding the matter of a Sanhedrin, this has already been debated in many places, and some of those who initially proposed or agreed to it have retracted their support. However, there are those who now propose a "quasi-Sanhedrin," not a literal Sanhedrin. A similar proposal existed in the previous generation, and the Gedolei Yisrael nullified it completely. Those who are small (in yiras shamayim) know that their voice will not be heard in the camp of Israel, for only the word of Hashem is a lamp to their feet. They will not listen to the voice of those who speak disparagingly of the leaders of Israel who follow the path of Torah and mitzvos, who fight its battles, strengthen its breaches, and compel Israel to walk in it. And even if they come to them in the name of a party, a program, or a partnership of the name of Heaven with other things, etc.—they will surely drive them out of the camp.

And if in that generation they nullified the matter for known reasons, then how much more so in our orphaned generation. And the wise will understand.

One sees tangibly that even among those who support the aforementioned proposal, the less a certain circle possesses the quality of yiras shamayim, the greater their enthusiasm for the idea of a Sanhedrin, or something like a Sanhedrin.

**Regarding Letter 4.** Tzitzis by machine, and likewise matzos. My father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory (the father of my father-in-law, the Rebbe), instructed in practice—in a time of distress, when refugees were numerous in Russia (in the year 5670 and onwards)—to make tzitzis by machine, provided that the beginning of the process was done by a person; but this is not the place to elaborate.

The opinions of the Acharonim regarding machine-made tzitzis are collected in *Me'asef LeChol HaMachanos*, ch. 11, sub-ch. 3, and in *Halacha L'Moshe* on the *Kitzur Shulchan Aruch*, beginning of ch. 9.

Regarding machine-made matzah, see *Sdei Chemed*, *Ma'areches Chometz U'Matzah*, ch. 13; in *Haggadah HaMevo'ar* (Piotrków, 5664); and *Sha'arim HaMetzuyanim B'Halacha* on the *Kitzur Shulchan Aruch*, sub-ch. 10, at the end.

## 3783

B"H, 29 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi, G-d-fearing, learned, and diligent in Torah,
Rabbi Avraham Yisrael Moshe S.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter regarding the tractates of the Talmud Yerushalmi: For the time being, I have received only one volume, tractate Berachos, and not the volumes of Peah, Demai, and Maaser Sheni that you mentioned in your letter.

May it be the will of Hashem that He grant success to your efforts to disseminate the study of the Talmud Yerushalmi. As is already known, the Rebbe the Mitteler Rebbe, son of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe, author of the Tanya and Shulchan Aruch, wrote regarding the Talmud Yerushalmi; this has been printed in Shaarei Orah (published by Kehot, pages 44-47, and elsewhere).

With blessings for success to you and your colleagues, the Rabbis, shlita.

P.S.
Enclosed is a check for the shipping expenses.

As a matter of interest, I have noted down, albeit in haste, a few comments on the *Kuntres Chiddushei HaRa'im* found at the end of the volume.

**At the beginning:** Regarding the statement, "If we say that Krias Shema is Rabbinic—for Rabbinic enactments we do not establish a safeguard, etc."—see the Bavli (4b): "The Sages established a safeguard for their words, etc., [regarding] Maariv prayer."
- Regarding the conclusion of the paragraph, one should note the Sha'agat Aryeh, section 1.

**There, at the beginning of the chapter:** One must contemplate what the connection is between the two, etc., and it is not explicitly stated whether, etc. Both matters are explained in the Tosefta (though in the Yerushalmi it is brought briefly). The text reads (Tosefta, Chapter 3, 21; Bavli 31a): "One should not stand to pray, etc., and likewise one should not take leave, etc., for we find that the early prophets concluded their words with words of praise and consolation." The proof from the conclusion of the prophets obviously applies also to "one should not take leave," as that too is a conclusion of the matter. Indeed, it is even more straightforward than the proof for prayer (see the Ritva in the Alfasi). Based on this, it is understood that "remembering Him" is a separate matter, and this is explicitly stated in the Ritva there. The Gra emends the Bavli regarding the explanation of "one should not take leave, etc., except through the joy of a mitzvah." In the Tosefta, the text is also identical regarding prayer and taking leave of one's friend: "a word of wisdom."

**At the end:** Rabbi Meir says: "A person of Yisrael is surrounded by mitzvos; in the bathhouse, he is 'naked' of mitzvos until he looks at the milah." The author raises a difficulty: according to the opinion that it is permitted to contemplate words of Torah in a lavatory, one should be able to contemplate words of Torah [in the bathhouse], and he discusses this at length. However, there is no difficulty from the outset, for Rabbi Meir’s intent is to explain the merit of every individual of Yisrael, who are surrounded by mitzvos, even at a time when he is not intending or occupied with them at all. See Menachos 43b: "The Holy One, Blessed be He, surrounded them [with mitzvos]." There is no room for the aforementioned difficulty. According to the approach of the Kuntres, it would have been better to raise the difficulty that surely, even in the bathhouse, the mitzvos of the "duties of the heart" would be fulfilled, such as the commandment of Hashem to unify Him, to love Him, and to fear Him, etc. Furthermore, in his very utterance, "Woe is me that I am 'naked' of mitzvos in the bathhouse," he is fulfilling the mitzvah of the fear of Hashem (and his faith). This is simple.

## 3784

B”H, 29 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 24 Tammuz: it is a pity that you wait to write until there is some matter of distress, Heaven forbid. Given the concept of ahavas Yisrael—which is one of the rational mitzvos, and fundamentally, as Rabbi Akiva says, a "great principle of the Torah" and the foundation of the entire Torah, and as Hillel the Elder says, and as is explained in Tanya, Chapter 32, and in the Tzemach Tzedek’s Sefer HaMitzvos regarding the mitzvah of ahavas Yisrael—you should be writing letters when there are joyous occasions. By doing so, the need for letters of the aforementioned type would diminish...

Regarding what you write at the end of your letter, that "I am working very hard, etc., in the gemach in the name of our holy ones, and I am managing it," it is understood that there is a contradiction here between the beginning and the end. For work in a gemach cannot be "hard," since through it one receives the greatest spiritual satisfaction. We see tangibly that "hard work" is work that is against one’s will, even if it is very light, while work performed out of pleasure and inner desire adds health to the soul and adds health to the body. If you will contemplate, even for a short while, what is said, "He who lends to Hashem is gracious to the poor," and the statement of Chazal that "gemach is greater than tzedakah," you will rejoice in your good portion every day—that Divine Providence has chosen you to help Bnei Yisrael in their time of need. May it be His will that for long days and good years, you will succeed in the pillar of gemach in service of the community, in addition to the fact that you are surely occupied with the three pillars of Torah, avodah, and gemach as an individual as well.

As you requested, I will mention all those you wrote about at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, and may Hashem grant you success in bringing good tidings regarding all of this.

With blessings.

B"H, 29 Tammuz, 5715
Brooklyn

As is known, my father-in-law, the Rebbe, noted in the famous Iggeres HaKodesh, Epistle 22 (134b), that he wrote the title "Av" (Father) in both instances, and he was precise: when it is in the manner of "he who loves him disciplines him with mussar," the title "Chossid" is not used. However, when he acts as an advocate for merit, so that it should be specifically in a way that is good, revealed and apparent, he adds this title as well.

Enclosed is the booklet of the continuation of the maamar that was published for the days of Geulah, 12 and 13 Tammuz—our redemption and the salvation of our souls. Surely you will merit to share its contents with the many, and the merit of the many depends upon you.

*[Tammuz, 5715]*

#### ***3786***

Published in *Likkutei Sichos*, Vol. 22, p. 297.

**Necessitated:** A response to a question regarding Rashi in *Sukkah* (53a, bottom): "David would not render a halachic decision in the presence of his teacher, Achitophel, and he [Achitophel] was there." What compelled Rashi to explain that David knew [the law] but did not wish to render a decision, rather than interpreting the text according to its literal, straightforward meaning?

## 3787

*[Tammuz, 5715]*

**Pure water:** Even though, by their nature, they are fit to be used for immersion (unlike impure or invalid water—at least according to Rabbinic law—where we require that the process of its formation be through a state of purity).

This is not as implied in the sefer *Mishnah Acharonah*. Furthermore, what is written in *Tiferes Yisrael* regarding the law of *hechsher tumah* (susceptibility to impurity) does not seem to me to have any relevance here. See also the sefer *Gulas Aliyos*; *Prishah* to *Yoreh Deah*, siman 24, regarding the concept of *hagramah*; and *Sdei Chemed*, *Kelalim*, letter *Beis*, subsection 1-2.

***

**Pure water:** A response to a question regarding *Mikvaos*, chapter 7, mishnah 2: "And these invalidate, but do not render fit, the water—whether they are impure or pure." The novelty here is regarding pure or impure water.

## 3,288

B"H, 1 Menachem Av, 5715
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed and distinguished, etc., R' [Aharon] shlita,

Shalom u'Bracha!

I acknowledge receipt of your letter from the 17th of Tammuz, and thank you for sending the photograph of the students of the Oholei Yosef Yitzchak Lubavitch schools at their summer camp.

Thank you for the good news regarding the farbrengens held on the Chag HaGeulah of 12th and 13th of Tammuz—the redemption of the Nasi of Yisrael and our own redemption and the salvation of our souls. Surely you are striving to ensure that this hisorerus continues throughout the entire year and translates into practical action, in accordance with the Mishnah’s teaching: "It is not the study that is the main thing, but the action."

Regarding what you wrote about Mr. ... shlita from the city of Bashar, who told you about what is happening with his son ... shlita: It was necessary to explain to him that this is, chas v'shalom, not a matter of "demons" (r"l), but purely a matter of physical health. Since physical health is dependent upon spiritual matters, and subsequently one must also act through natural means, he should have the mezuzos in his home checked, as well as his tefillin. My intention is not the exterior of the tefillin batim, but primarily that the parshiyos be kosher according to law. The boy’s mother should be careful in matters of tznius, and especially in the laws and statutes of taharas hamishpachah. Before lighting candles every Erev Shabbos and Erev Yom Tov, and also every Monday and Thursday morning, she should set aside a few coins for tzedakah. Their son should wear a tallis katan and be careful to keep his head covered. In addition to this, they should seek the advice of a specialist physician in this field in a large city, and follow his instructions. May the Ribono shel Olam grant them the merit and success to raise him for Torah, chuppah, and maasim tovim, and may he be whole.

Regarding what you wrote about Mr. ... shlita, who has an ailment in the hair of his eyes, etc.—he should seek the advice of a specialist physician and follow his instructions. He should also have his tefillin checked, and from now on, he should be careful with Kiddush and Havdalah.

As you requested, all of these will be mentioned at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, ztzk"l, nbg"m, zy"a, Nasi of Yisrael, for whatever they may need. Surely, when you have good news regarding this, you will inform [us]...

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe shlita,
Secretary

## 3,185

B"H, 1 Menachem Av, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 21 Tammuz, in which you write regarding your current work—that it is difficult, even though your livelihood is provided through it—and therefore the thought has occurred to you to accept a teaching position in... even though you have not previously attempted such work.

Now, one of the main points is missing from your letter: whether this question of yours is with the agreement of your wife, tichyeh. For in worldly matters, one must consult with her, as our Sages of blessed memory have stated, and especially since this is a general matter concerning the livelihood of the entire household. If she also agrees to place this question on the agenda, it would be worthwhile to test your strength in matters of education, as there is certainly room for this trial even during the vacation period, **without** any commitment on your part...

It is surprising that you do not mention anything regarding your *shiurim* in the study of *Nigleh* and *Chassidus*, nor your participation in communal activities in the vineyard of Chabad, for this is the channel and the *keilim*—especially for *Anash*—to receive the blessings of Hashem, may He be blessed, in all that they require.

With blessings.

## 3,192

B"H, 2 Menachem Av, 5715
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and distinguished man of many accomplishments, engaged in communal needs,
Mr. A. Levenstein, may he live.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of the 23rd of Tammuz: It is a pleasure for me to read of your efforts and success in the affairs of the Charedi community in your area, and specifically in the field of kosher education. If, at the time when the Beis Hamikdash was standing, they said and emphasized, "If there are no kids, there are no goats," then how much more so in the time of Galus and in our generation—this orphaned generation—is the question of the education of the younger generation a vital issue and the foundation for the existence of our entire nation, Am Yisrael. Every effort and endeavor in this regard, even if it requires strength, energy, and time, is worthwhile, especially since we have been promised that there is a strength in propaganda [outreach] that does not return empty. When one acts with truth, Hashem Yisbarach grants success, and even beyond the natural order.

Therefore, I was pleased to read in the continuation of your letter regarding the development of the school, which provides the opportunity for Jewish children to be in a Charedi environment, to add significantly more hours to the study of Kodesh than until now. I was happy to read this not only as one who shares in the joy of his friend and peer, but also as per the statement of our Sages, "All of Israel are responsible for one another." From this, it is understood that as much as there is an increase in Yiras Shamayim, in the study of Torah, and in the fulfillment of Mitzvos in one community of Israel, it adds strength and power to all the communities of the children of Israel scattered to the four corners of the earth, and "each will help his fellow and say to his brother, 'Be strong.'"

With respect and with a blessing for success in your holy work, to increase education in the spirit of the Israel of old, and with regards to all your colleagues in this.

## 3,393

B"H, 2 Menachem Av, 5715
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed avreich, G-d-fearing, refined, and diligent in communal needs,
R' Shalom Dov, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of the 25th of Tammuz... It is understood and simple that you must increase your courage in your work of expanding holy education, without taking into account disturbances and opponents. It is best to keep in mind the points from my previous letters—namely, not to dissipate your energies on matters that are not your primary and essential task. May it be the will of Hashem that the desire of Hashem shall succeed in your hands to increase Torah with pure yiras shamayim, for then, when studied in a manner of *zachah* (merit), it becomes a potion of life.

Regarding what you wrote about the *batim* (tefillin boxes) being two-by-two [fingerbreadths], see the sefer *Shiurei Torah* by Rabbi Naeh, of blessed memory. It is known that our custom is in accordance with this measurement, and it is also mentioned in *Tanya*, *Iggeres HaKodesh*, section 10 (115a).

Regarding the *yahrtzeit* studies on Tisha B'Av: Before midday, the study of Mishnayos after davening consists of the well-known chapters of *Taanis* and *Moed Katan*. After midday, the custom is the same as with all other shiurim—that is, one who is engaged in them throughout the year maintains them on Tisha B'Av as well. See *Hayom Yom* for this day.

Regarding visiting the cemetery: Although it is mentioned in the books of halachic instruction, I did not see my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, conduct himself in this manner. Perhaps one of the reasons is that it is impossible to immerse in a mikveh on this day beforehand. I found a similar point in *Nimukei Orach Chaim* 559 by the Rebbe of Munkatch. As for what is written there that some go and stand outside, I did not see my father-in-law, the Rebbe, conduct himself in this manner either. This can also be explained based on what is written in various places, that even through looking and seeing at the cemetery, some form of connection is created, and consequently, this too should be linked to the lack of the aforementioned immersion...

With blessings and regards to all his friends, may they live.

***

3,393

## 3295

3295

B.H., 3 Menachem Av, 5715
Brooklyn

To the members and congregants of the Kehilas... Synagogue,
May they live.

Greetings and blessings!

I was pleased to receive regards from you and from your synagogue through the esteemed Rabbi, the G-d-fearing man of action, who is involved in communal needs with great energy, etc., [Name], who also told me about the classes being taught in public. May Hashem Yisbarach grant success so that the synagogue is conducted according to the law of our Torah, the Torah of life, in which we constantly increase in matters of Torah and mitzvos—in the words of our Sages, "we ascend in holiness." The Torah also promises that whoever adds, they add to him; this brings an increase of blessing from Hashem Yisbarach for the members and congregants of the synagogue, in all that they and their households need, both materially and spiritually.

In my conversation with the Rabbi, shlita, regarding the fact that a synagogue is a *mikdash me'at* (a miniature sanctuary) and must therefore contain some of the holiness that was in the Beis Hamikdash, and that it is therefore necessary to have a high *mechitzah* in the synagogue, I was surprised to learn from his words that in this particular detail, some of the synagogue's congregants feel a sense of hesitation.

Although I am certain that this hesitation stems only from the fact that the seriousness of the matter—and how much it affects, sooner or later, the entire existence of the synagogue and the well-being of the congregants—has not been sufficiently explained, I am sure that the Rabbi, shlita, as well as the other congregants, will find the appropriate words to explain the above and lead everyone to a firm decision that there must be a *mechitzah* according to the law and with *hiddur* (beautification).

Nevertheless, I also wish to write a few lines here for additional reinforcement:

In many matters, the situation is seen more clearly when one relates it to oneself in material affairs. Imagine that a person of flesh and blood needs a favor in a material matter from another person, even if the matter is not strictly necessary for the one requesting it. While it is true that one person is not one hundred percent dependent on the opinion of another, and can find someone higher than him, or can generally survive without the other's favor, it is understood and simple to everyone that when he approaches him to speak about the favor he wants him to do, he will not begin by insulting him, angering him, or doing things contrary to his will, and then immediately tell him that now—after those actions—he wants him to do the favor for which he came to him.

A synagogue is a house of prayer where Jews gather with the faith that the Ribono shel Olam is the Creator of the world and its Guide, that He is the One who sustains and provides for all, the Healer of all flesh, etc. When each person wants to be sure that he and his household will have the sustenance they need, and all other matters they require, and they know that everything depends on Hashem, they come to the synagogue and ask Hashem Yisbarach: "Hear our voice, spare us and have mercy upon us, and accept our prayer with mercy and favor." But this is when one stands to request this while acting in a manner contrary to the will of Hashem, as He revealed to us in His holy Torah! And where does he do this? In a place called a house of prayer, saying: "You commanded that the synagogue be conducted in a set order and that prayers be said in the appropriate manner," yet we are not moved by this, and we show You a "trick" (*a kuntz*)—that despite all this, we will act contrary to Your will and to anger You, and then we will have the audacity to say, "Heal us and we will be healed," "Bless this year for us," "Bless us, our Father."

I am confident that after a little reflection on all that has been said above, there will not be even a shred of doubt regarding the synagogue in general, and regarding the law of the *mechitzah* in particular.

In these days, when we mourn the destruction of the Beis Hamikdash and the exile—as we say, "Because of our sins we were exiled from our land," from which it is also understood that the redemption depends on the rectification of our sins—may Hashem Yisbarach grant each and every one of us, within the entirety of Klal Yisrael, an increase in vitality in the performance of mitzvos with *hiddur*, even in private matters, and all the more so in public matters, which is also a *Kiddush Hashem* in public. Through this, we hasten the true and complete redemption through Moshiach Tzidkeinu, speedily in our days.

With blessings to each and every one of you, that you may live in both material and spiritual matters.

## 3,796

B"H, 5 Menachem Av, 5715
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 28 Tammuz, in which you write that after receiving my letter you were inspired and maintained that inspiration for some time in your learning, davening, and conduct, but that subsequently you weakened in this, and since then you have been experiencing ups and downs, and consequently, feelings of despair are growing within you, etc.

Now, I have already written to several avreichim who find themselves in such a state, that their situation is addressed in many places in the holy Tanya, including Chapter 27 and onwards. In general, the trait of despair and the seeking of miracles specifically in the war against the yetzer hara, etc., is nothing but one of the enticements and incitements of the yetzer hara. As with all the tactics of the yetzer hara, the most excellent path is not to enter into a debate with him in the first place, but rather to strengthen oneself in the three pillars—Torah, avodah, and the performance of mitzvos in general—and gemilus chasadim in abundance and with vigor. Then, you are guaranteed that the darkness will diminish and be pushed away. It is a pity to waste time on the pilpul regarding this. Furthermore, when he also utilizes his power of influence on those younger than himself to bring them closer to the service of Hashem, his own siyata d'shmaya will also increase.

I trust that you are keeping the three daily shiurim that are incumbent upon every soul, as established by my father-in-law, the Rebbe, ztzel, nbgm, zy"a—the well-known Chumash, Tehillim, and Tanya—and that you give a coin or two to tzedakah every weekday before davening. May Hashem grant you success.

With blessings.

## 3997

3997

By the grace of Hashem, 5 Menachem Av, 5715
Brooklyn.

To the G-d-fearing, learned, and distinguished man of many accomplishments, whose reputation precedes him,
Rabbi Aryeh, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

I have heard of you from others on several occasions, and I was pleased to learn of your good deeds, primarily in the vein of *ahavas Yisrael*. As explained in *Tanya*, Chapter 32, and at length in the *Tzemach Tzedek’s* *Sefer HaMitzvos* regarding the mitzvah of *ahavas Yisrael*, this conduct has an immeasurable impact both below and above. Happy is his portion that Divine Providence has granted him the opportunity to conduct himself in accordance with this. And it is certain that, ultimately, through *ahavas Yisrael*, one also effects an increase in *ahavas HaTorah* and *ahavas Hashem*. I refer here to those with whom he comes into contact through this conduct, in whom the point [of holiness] is ultimately awakened, resulting in an increase in their love for Hashem and their love for the Torah—which exists within every single Jew. And although it is simple, as our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe writes in *Tanya*, that even if "they did not [reciprocate], he has not lost the reward for the mitzvah of loving one's fellow," we see tangibly that—aside from exceptional cases and the rare minority—when the closeness fostered by *ahavas Yisrael* is strengthened, one sometimes mentions matters concerning Hashem, His Torah, and His mitzvos, and the words are heard and also produce their effect over time. May the Ribono shel Olam grant you success in seeing good fruit from your labor, without a lapse of time between the sowing and the reaping.

I was very pleased with what you wrote in your letter regarding the saying of the Tzemach Tzedek concerning Aharon the High Priest and Michael the High Priest. *Milta al-bishaihu yakira* (a precious matter). This also relates to the aforementioned, as explained in *Tanya*, Chapter 32, "Be of the disciples of Aharon, etc.," and as explained at length in *Avos D'Rabbi Nosson* there.

Regarding what you wrote at the end of your letter—that you possess an old manuscript containing a *maamar* of the Tzemach Tzedek concerning the Baal Shem Tov, the Maggid, and the Alter Rebbe—while I have not heard this story, the words are worthy of being said, even if not everyone has the ability or the license to say them. *Rav cheilei*—it must be, *u'guvrai* (his strength is great, and his men).

Since, as mentioned, I have not heard this from my father-in-law, the Rebbe, *ztzukulleh"h, nbg"m, zy"a*, and furthermore, I have not yet found it recorded among the manuscripts, it is not within my power to state or verify the story. However, as noted, it is possible that such things were said by the Tzemach Tzedek.

It is certainly permissible to request it, and if you find such precious matters as mentioned above, and you would send a copy here, my thanks are given in advance.

With blessings for great success in your *avodah* for the sake of Heaven.

Regarding the Baal Shem Tov and Eliyahu HaNavi—in reference to what you mentioned in the aforementioned old manuscript, it is worth noting the story told by the Rebbe, the father of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, which states: "The Baal Shem Tov said that he had the power to ascend in a whirlwind to the heavens like Eliyahu, but 'I wish to pass through the [reality of] "for dust you are and to dust you shall return,"' for just as the *his-havus* (emergence) of dust is derived specifically from *Atzmus* (the Essence), so too, within the level of 'dust' there is also present the power of *Atzmus* (*Toras Shalom*, p. 50. See there)."

## 3998

B"H, 5 Menachem Av, 5715
Brooklyn

To the Administration of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad in the Holy City of Jerusalem, may it be rebuilt and established,
May they live,

Peace and blessing!

I acknowledge receipt of your letter from the 23rd of Tammuz. It is surprising—as I have written to you previously—that you are so brief in your writing. There are long periods where you do not write at all, and after such an interval, when you do write, it is in a very abbreviated manner. This is the case with this letter as well, where you write only in bullet points. Yet, the Chabad approach is well known: it must be specifically through *hasagah* (intellectual grasp) and *havanah* (understanding), which are achieved by holding onto the details.

However, one must guard the "point"—*nekudah b'heichala*—so that one does not wander onto a crooked path. This point is the matter of *yirah* (awe), *kabbalas ol* (acceptance of the yoke), and *achdus* (unity) in their absolute sense, and in their proper order in a person's *avodah*. And even though this *yirah* is seemingly *yirah tata'ah* (lower-level awe), it is explained in many places how it relates to *yirah ila'ah* (higher-level awe). Furthermore, through *kabbalas ol* one arrives at *achdus*, as is brought in *Tanya*, Chapter 32. All three of these are the *Yud* and the *kotzo shel Yud* (the tip of the *Yud*), which is the "point within the palace."

Enclosed is a copy regarding the utilization of vacation days, even though, in general, my perspective is known: that now is not the time for vacation days at all. On the contrary—and for several reasons—if I had the strength, I would have abolished them in the past, and certainly in the present and the future.

With blessings for success in your holy work and for good tidings regarding all of the above.
